Distinction Between Sahw and Nisyan with Philosophical Objection
Al-Amidi defines sahw and nisyan, presents an objection, and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise distinction between sahw and nisyan according to the mutakallimun, and how a philosophical objection regarding the active intellect is addressed.

Core Idea
Sahw and nisyan differ in which mental faculty loses the form.

Theologians distinguish between inadvertence (sahw) and forgetting (nisyan) based on the two faculties of perception and memory. However, this distinction faced a philosophical objection from those who posit an active intellect as the repository of intelligibles. Al-Āmidī responds by unifying several terms and clarifying their opposition to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of sahw and nisyan
Identify the two faculties involved and what happens in each case.
The key difference: in sahw, the form remains in memory; in nisyan, it is lost from both.
2
Examine the philosophical objection
The objection claims that on the philosophers' view, the active intellect cannot lose forms, so nisyan would be impossible. Understand why.
3
Analyze al-Amidi's response
Al-Amidi argues that ghafla, dhuhul, and nisyan are essentially the same and all are opposite to knowledge in the sense of incompatibility.
He also clarifies that this opposition is not in the technical sense of mutually exclusive opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد فرق بين السهو والنسيان بأن الأول : زوال الصورة عن المدركة مع بقائها في الحافظة، والثاني : زوالها عنهما معاً، فيحتاج حينئذ قوله: ( والثاني زوالها عنهما إن قيل : الفرق المذكور للفلاسفة وحافظة النفس الداركة للمعقولات وخزائنتها عندهم هي العقل الفعال ولا يتصور زوال الصورة عنه على قاعدتهم في حصولها إلى سبب جديد قال الآمدي : إِنَّ الغفلة والذهول والنسيان عبارات مختلفة لكن يقرب أن تكون معانيها ،متحدة، وكلها مضادة للعلم بمعنى أنه يستحيل اجتماعها معه قوله: ( بمعنى أن يستحيل إلخ) لا بالمعنى المصطلح لعدم كونها وجودية قال : والجهل البسيط يمتنع اجتماعه مع العلم لذاتيهما فيكون ضداً له، وإن لم تكن صفة إثبات وليس أي الجهل البسيط ضداً للجهل المركب، ولا للشك ولا للظن ولا للنظر بل يجامع كلاً منها لكنه يضاد النوم والغفلة والموت لأنه عدم العلم من شأنه أن يقوم به العلم وذلك غير متصور في حالة النوم وأخواته، وأما العلم فإنه جميع هذه الأمور المذكورة.
And there is a distinction between sahw and nisyān: the first is the removal of the form (ṣūra) from the perceiving faculty (al-mudrika) while it remains in the preserving faculty (al-ḥāfiẓa), and the second is its removal from both. So it is needed then. His saying: (The second is its removal from both). If it is said: This distinction belongs to the philosophers, and the preserving faculty of the soul that perceives intelligibles and its treasuries according to them is the active intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), and it is inconceivable that the form should be removed from it according to their principle of its occurrence from a new cause. Al-Āmidī said: Indeed, forgetfulness (ghafla), heedlessness (dhuhūl), and forgetfulness (nisyān) are different expressions but their meanings are likely united, and all of them are opposed to knowledge in the sense that it is impossible for them to coexist with it. His saying: (in the sense that it is impossible etc.) not in the technical sense because they are not positive (wujūdiyya). He said: Simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basīṭ) is impossible to coexist with knowledge due to their essences, so it is a contrary (ḍidd) to it, even if it is not a positive attribute. And simple ignorance is not a contrary to compound ignorance, nor to doubt (shakk), nor to opinion (ẓann), nor to speculation (naẓar); rather, it can coexist with each of them, but it opposes sleep (nawm), forgetfulness (ghafla), and death (mawt) because it is the absence of knowledge in something that is apt to have knowledge, and that is inconceivable in a state of sleep and its likes. As for knowledge, it embraces all these mentioned matters.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the distinction between sahw (inadvertence) and nisyān (forgetting): in sahw, the form (concept) is lost from the perceiving faculty but remains in the preserving faculty (memory); in nisyān, it is lost from both. The commentary then presents an objection against this distinction from the perspective of the philosophers (falāsifa). The objection argues that the preserving faculty for intelligibles is the active intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), and on their principles, a form from the active intellect cannot be removed because it requires a new cause. Therefore, nisyān would be impossible. Al-Āmidī responds by arguing that ghafla, dhuhūl, and nisyān are essentially the same in meaning and all are opposed to knowledge in the sense that they cannot coexist with it. The gloss clarifies that this opposition is not in the technical sense of mutually exclusive opposites because these states are non-existences (ʿadam). Then al-Āmidī continues: simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) cannot coexist with knowledge due to their essences, so it is a contrary (ḍidd) to knowledge, even though it is not a positive attribute. However, simple ignorance is not a contrary to compound ignorance, doubt, opinion, or speculation; it can coexist with them. But it does oppose sleep, ghafla, and death, because in those states the capacity for knowledge is absent. The passage concludes by noting that knowledge encompasses all these states.

النسيان
المدركة
الحافظة
العقل الفعال
الآمدي
الغفلة
الذهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sahw and nisyan are identical and both involve complete loss.
Correction: In sahw, the form remains in memory; in nisyan it is lost from both.

Checkpoint
According to the distinction given, what is the difference between sahw and nisyan?
A) In sahw the form is lost from both faculties; in nisyan it remains in memory
B) In sahw the form remains in the perceiving faculty; in nisyan it is lost from both
C) In sahw the form is lost from the perceiving faculty but remains in memory; in nisyan it is lost from both
D) In sahw the form is lost from memory but remains in perception; in nisyan it is lost from both

Key Takeaway
Sahw and nisyan are distinguished by whether the form remains in memory, and the philosophical objection about the active intellect is answered by al-Amidi's unification and clarification of opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself forgetting Allah in your daily life, do you recognize it as sahw (a momentary lapse of attention while the memory remains) or nisyan (a complete loss of remembrance)? How does this distinction force you to take responsibility for your spiritual state right now?
?
The philosophers argued that the active intellect cannot forget, so nisyan is impossible. Do you ever use intellectual excuses to avoid admitting your own heedlessness? Will you today honestly examine yourself and repent for the moments you claimed you 'couldn't help' forgetting Allah?

Simple Ignorance as a Contrary to Knowledge
Al-Amidi discusses simple ignorance as a contrary to knowledge and its relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of simple ignorance (jahl basit) as a contrary to knowledge, and how it relates to other epistemic states.

Core Idea
Simple ignorance directly opposes knowledge but coexists with doubt and opinion.

Al-Amidi extends his discussion of opposites to address simple ignorance. He argues that simple ignorance is a contrary (didd) to knowledge due to their essences, even though it is not a positive attribute. He further clarifies which states it opposes and which it does not, providing a nuanced view of epistemic opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of simple ignorance as a contrary
Simple ignorance (jahl basit) is the absence of knowledge in something that can know. It cannot coexist with knowledge.
This is based on their essences.
2
Examine what simple ignorance does NOT oppose
It does not oppose compound ignorance, doubt, opinion, or speculation; it can coexist with them.
3
Understand what simple ignorance does oppose
It opposes sleep, ghafla (forgetfulness), and death, because in those states the capacity for knowledge is absent.
The passage concludes that knowledge encompasses all these states.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So it is needed then. His saying: (The second is its removal from both). If it is said: This distinction belongs to the philosophers, and the preserving faculty of the soul that perceives intelligibles and its treasuries according to them is the active intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), and it is inconceivable that the form should be removed from it according to their principle of its occurrence from a new cause. Al-Āmidī said: Indeed, forgetfulness (ghafla), heedlessness (dhuhūl), and forgetfulness (nisyān) are different expressions but their meanings are likely united, and all of them are opposed to knowledge in the sense that it is impossible for them to coexist with it. His saying: (in the sense that it is impossible etc.) not in the technical sense because they are not positive (wujūdiyya). He said: Simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basīṭ) is impossible to coexist with knowledge due to their essences, so it is a contrary (ḍidd) to it, even if it is not a positive attribute. And simple ignorance is not a contrary to compound ignorance, nor to doubt (shakk), nor to opinion (ẓann), nor to speculation (naẓar); rather, it can coexist with each of them, but it opposes sleep (nawm), forgetfulness (ghafla), and death (mawt) because it is the absence of knowledge in something that is apt to have knowledge, and that is inconceivable in a state of sleep and its likes. As for knowledge, it embraces all these mentioned matters.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the distinction between sahw (inadvertence) and nisyān (forgetting): in sahw, the form (concept) is lost from the perceiving faculty but remains in the preserving faculty (memory); in nisyān, it is lost from both. The commentary then presents an objection against this distinction from the perspective of the philosophers (falāsifa). The objection argues that the preserving faculty for intelligibles is the active intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), and on their principles, a form from the active intellect cannot be removed because it requires a new cause. Therefore, nisyān would be impossible. Al-Āmidī responds by arguing that ghafla, dhuhūl, and nisyān are essentially the same in meaning and all are opposed to knowledge in the sense that they cannot coexist with it. The gloss clarifies that this opposition is not in the technical sense of mutually exclusive opposites because these states are non-existences (ʿadam). Then al-Āmidī continues: simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) cannot coexist with knowledge due to their essences, so it is a contrary (ḍidd) to knowledge, even though it is not a positive attribute. However, simple ignorance is not a contrary to compound ignorance, doubt, opinion, or speculation; it can coexist with them. But it does oppose sleep, ghafla, and death, because in those states the capacity for knowledge is absent. The passage concludes by noting that knowledge encompasses all these states.

Correction: Simple ignorance only opposes knowledge, not compound ignorance or doubt; it can coexist with them.

Key Takeaway
Simple ignorance (jahl basit) is a contrary to knowledge but not to other epistemic states; it opposes only those states where knowledge is impossible, like sleep and death.

Why Simple Ignorance Opposes Doubt but Not Forgetfulness
Explains the subtle difference in opposition between jahl basit and states like ghafla vs. shakk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why simple ignorance (jahl basit) is considered to oppose doubt (shakk) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) but not forgetfulness (ghafla), based on the soul's orientation toward the denied knowledge.

Core Idea
Simple ignorance opposes only states where the soul is oriented toward knowledge.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle point about how simple ignorance relates to different mental states. While it is clear that simple ignorance does not occur in sleep or death, its relationship with forgetfulness (ghafla) versus doubt (shakk) is more nuanced. The key lies in whether the soul is oriented toward the knowledge that is absent—a distinction that shapes the boundaries of these concepts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline: sleep and death
The passage first states that simple ignorance is inconceivable in sleep and death, as the subject lacks the capacity for knowledge entirely. This sets the foundation for comparing other states.
This is the clear part of the argument.
2
Identify the problem: ghafla vs. shakk
The difficulty arises when considering ghafla (forgetfulness as lack of attention) versus shakk (doubt) and jahl murakkab (compound ignorance). The author admits there is obscurity here.
3
Focus on the distinction: orientation of the soul
The proposed distinction is that in shakk and jahl murakkab, the soul is oriented (tawajjuh) toward the denied knowledge, whereas in ghafla it is not. This orientation allows the potential for knowledge to occur.
The term 'tawajjuh al-nafs' (orientation of the soul) is key.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by noting that the matter is still subject to reflection (ta'ammul), indicating this is a nuanced point open to further thought.
This humility invites deeper study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وذلك غير متصور في حالة النوم وأخواته عدم كونه متصوراً في حالة النوم والموت ظاهر في الجملة، وأما عدم كونه متصوراً في حالة الغفلة مع كونه متصوراً في حالة الشك والجهل المركب، ففيه ،خفاء، اللهم إلا أن يبنى الفرق على أنَّ في الشك والجهل المركب توجه النفس والتفاتها إلى نحو متعلق العلم المنفي، فمن شأن الشاك والجاهل جهلاً مركباً أن يقوم بهما ذلك العلم الذي لا يمكن بدون الالتفات بخلاف الغافل والذاهل والكلام بعد محل تأمل .
His saying: (And that is inconceivable in the state of sleep and its likes). The fact that it is not conceivable in the state of sleep and death is evident in general. As for it not being conceivable in the state of forgetfulness (ghafla) while it is conceivable in the state of doubt (shakk) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), there is obscurity therein – except that the distinction may be built on the fact that in doubt and compound ignorance there is an orientation (tawajjuh) of the soul and its attention toward the direction of the denied knowledge. So it is the nature of the doubter and the one compoundly ignorant to have that knowledge – which is impossible without attention – occur in them, unlike the forgetful (ghāfil) and the heedless (dhāhil). And the matter is still subject to reflection.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a gloss that examines a subtle point about the opposition of simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) to various states. It was previously stated that simple ignorance is not conceivable in sleep and death because the subject lacks the capacity for knowledge entirely. That is clear. However, the question arises: simple ignorance is also said to be not conceivable in ghafla (forgetfulness as lack of attention) but conceivable in shakk (doubt) and jahl murakkab (compound ignorance). Why is that? The author admits there is obscurity (khafā') here. He then offers a possible distinction: in doubt and compound ignorance, the soul is oriented (tawajjuh) and attentive toward the direction of the denied knowledge (i.e., the knowledge that is absent). Therefore, it is in the nature of the doubter and the compoundly ignorant to have that knowledge – which cannot occur without attention – potentially occur in them. In contrast, the forgetful (ghāfil) and heedless (dhāhil) lack this orientation. Hence, simple ignorance is not opposed to ghafla in the same way. The author concludes by noting that this issue is still subject to reflection (ta'ammul), indicating it is a nuanced point.

Opposites of Knowledge: Heedlessness, Forgetfulness, and Simple Ignorance
Al-Amidi defines opposites of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's classification of the opposites of knowledge and why simple ignorance is a true opposite yet can coexist with other cognitive states.

Core Idea
Not every lack of knowledge is an opposite; true opposition is defined by impossible coexistence.

Al-Amidi refines the concept of 'opposite' (ضد) in the context of knowledge. He distinguishes between simple ignorance and other states like heedlessness and forgetfulness, showing that simple ignorance truly opposes knowledge because they cannot coexist, yet it can coexist with compound ignorance or doubt. This precision is crucial for understanding how knowledge relates to its privations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'opposite'
Recognize that in kalam, two things are opposites if they cannot coexist and are mutually exclusive.
This definition is essential for the rest of the passage.
2
Identify the first three terms
Heedlessness (غفلة), absent-mindedness (ذهول), and forgetfulness (نسيان) are semantically similar and all oppose knowledge because they cannot coexist with it.
3
Examine simple ignorance
Simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) is the absence of knowledge in a subject capable of knowledge. It cannot coexist with knowledge, making it a true opposite, even though it is a privation, not a positive attribute.
This is a key point: opposition does not require both to be positive.
4
See what simple ignorance is NOT opposite to
Simple ignorance is not opposite to compound ignorance, doubt, conjecture, or reflection; in fact it can coexist with them.
The possessor of simple ignorance may be compoundly ignorant, a doubter, etc.
5
Identify what simple ignorance opposes
Simple ignorance is opposite to sleep, heedlessness, and death. These are states where the subject cannot have knowledge at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في حصولها إلى سبب جديد قال الآمدي : إِنَّ الغفلة والذهول والنسيان عبارات مختلفة لكن يقرب أن تكون معانيها متحدة، وكلها مضادة للعلم بمعنى أنه يستحيل اجتماعها معه قال : والجهل البسيط يمتنع اجتماعه مع العلم لذاتيهما فيكون ضداً له، وإن لم تكن صفة إثبات وليس أي الجهل البسيط ضداً للجهل المركب، ولا للشك ولا للظن ولا للنظر بل يجامع كلاً منها لكنه يضاد النوم والغفلة والموت لأنه عدم العلم من شأنه أن يقوم به العلم وذلك غير متصور في حالة النوم وأخواته، وأما العلم فإنه جميع هذه الأمور المذكورة.
قوله : ( بمعنى أن يستحيل إلخ) لا بالمعنى المصطلح لعدم كونها وجودية . قوله : (بل يجامع كلا منهما فإن صاحب الجهل البسيط إما جاهل جهلاً مركباً أو ظان أو شاك أو خال عن جميع أقسام الإدراك .
Regarding its occurrence due to a new cause, al-Amidi said: Indeed, heedlessness, absent-mindedness, and forgetfulness are different expressions but it is likely that their meanings are unified, and all of them are opposite to knowledge in the sense that it is impossible for them to coexist with it. He said: Simple ignorance cannot coexist with knowledge due to their essences, so it is opposite to it, even if it is not an affirmative attribute. Simple ignorance is not opposite to compound ignorance, nor to doubt, nor to conjecture, nor to reflection; rather it coexists with each of them, but it is opposite to sleep, heedlessness, and death because it is the absence of knowledge from something that is disposed to be the locus of knowledge, and that is inconceivable in the state of sleep and its likes. As for knowledge, it is opposite to all these mentioned matters. His saying: (meaning that it is impossible, etc.) not in the technical sense because they are not existential. His saying: (Rather it coexists with each of them, for the possessor of simple ignorance is either compoundly ignorant, or a conjecturer, or a doubter, or devoid of all types of perception.)
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of opposition (ضد) with respect to knowledge. Al-Amidi begins by stating that heedlessness, absent-mindedness, and forgetfulness are different terms but semantically unified, and they are contrary to knowledge because they cannot coexist with it. He then focuses on simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط), explaining that it cannot coexist with knowledge due to their essences, thus it is a true opposite even though it is a privation rather than a positive attribute. He clarifies that simple ignorance is not opposite to compound ignorance, doubt, conjecture, or reflection; in fact it can coexist with them. Instead, it is opposite to sleep, heedlessness, and death, because these states are the absence of knowledge from something inherently capable of having knowledge, while during sleep and similar states the subject cannot have knowledge. Finally, he states that knowledge is opposite to all the mentioned items. The commentator then adds that the phrase 'in the sense that it is impossible' refers to a non-technical meaning because these states are not existential. He also explains that the possessor of simple ignorance may be compoundly ignorant, a conjecturer, a doubter, or completely devoid of perception.

Correction: Al-Amidi explicitly states that simple ignorance is not opposite to compound ignorance; they can coexist.

Key Takeaway
Simple ignorance is a true opposite of knowledge because they cannot coexist, but it can coexist with compound ignorance, doubt, and conjecture.

Ash'ari Definition of Sensory Perception as Knowledge and the Majority Objection
Ash'ari definition of sensory perceptions as knowledge, majority objection, and reply with two possible differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that sensory perceptions are knowledge, the majority objection based on experiential difference, and the Ash'ari reply that the difference can be by type or identity, preserving the classification of sensory perception as knowledge.

Core Idea
Sensory perception is knowledge, differing from other knowledge by type or identity.

This passage addresses a key epistemological debate in Kalam: are the perceptions of the five external senses considered knowledge? Imam al-Ash'ari affirmed that they are, while the majority of theologians objected, citing an undeniable experiential difference between merely knowing an object and perceiving it with the senses. The Ash'ari response navigates this by allowing that sensory perception may be knowledge that differs from other knowledge in kind or in individual identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari definition
Note how Ash'ari equates each sense's perception with knowledge of its specific objects, and compares the senses to other means of acquiring knowledge like intuition and reflection.
2
Understand the majority objection
The objection points to an experiential difference between knowing something (e.g., a color) and seeing it. Grasp the force of this objection.
3
Examine the Ash'ari reply
The reply introduces two possibilities: the difference could be by type (النوع) or by identity (الهوية). Understand these concepts and how they preserve sensory perception as knowledge.
4
Follow the subsequent discussion
The passage anticipates an objection that the reply reduces the debate to a verbal one, and counters that difference in identity suffices. See the robustness of the reply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : إدراكات الحواس الظاهرة ] (إدراكات الحواس الخمس الظاهرة ( عند الشيخ ) الأشعري ( علم بمتعلقاتها فالسمع أي الإدراك بالسامعة (علم بالمسموعات والابصار) أي الإدراك بالباصرة (علم بالمبصرات ) وكذلك الحال في الإدراك باللامسة والذائقة والشامة، فهذه الحواس وسائل إلى تلك العلوم الحاصلة باستعمالها كالوجدان والبديهة والنظر التي يتوسل بها إلى العلوم المستندة إليها، وخالفه فيه الجمهور) من المتكلمين ( فإنا إذا علمنا شيئاً) كاللون مثلاً علماً تاماً، ثم رأيناه فإنا نجد بين الحالتين فرقاً ضرورياً)، ونعلم أنَّ الحالة الثانية مخالفة للحالة الأولى بلا شبهة، ولو كان الإبصار علماً بالمبصر لم يكن هناك فرق وهكذا نجد الفرق بين العلم بهذا الصوت وسماعه وبين العلم بهذا الطعم وذوقه وبين العلم بهذه الرائحة وشمها ( وله) أي للشيخ ( أن يجيب بأن ذلك الفرق ( الوجداني ( لا يمنع كونه ) أي كون إدراك الحواس علماً مخالفاً لسائر (العلوم المستندة إلى غير الحواس مخالفة (إما بالنوع أو بالهوية ، فيكون العلم على الأول حقيقة جنسية مشتملة على حقائق مختلفة منها إدراك الحواس، وعلى الثاني حقيقة نوعية متناولة لأفراد متخالفة بالهويات لا يقال : الخلاف إنما هو في أن حقيقة إدراك الشيء بإحدى الحواس هل هي حقيقة إدراكه المسمى بالعلم اتفاقاً ،أولا ، وإذا فرض اختلافهما بالنوع صار النزاع لفظياً راجعاً إلى أنَّ لفظ العلم اسم لمطلق الإدراك أو لنوع منه لأنا نقول : يكفينا في مقام المنع الاختلاف بالهوية لجواز استناد الفرق إليه، وذكر الاختلاف النوعي لمزيد الاستظهار ( وأيضاً فإنما يصح استدلاله ) أي استدلال الخصم أعني الجمهور ( لو أمكن العلم بمتعلقه )
قوله : ( كالوجدان والبديهة إلخ يعني كما أن هذه الثلاثة طرق ووسائل كذلك الحواس طرق والانكشاف في الجميع إنما هو للنفس فكون الحاصل بتلك الطرق علماً دون الحاصل بالحواس تحكم . قوله: (وللشيخ أن يجيب إلخ) خلاصته أن اختلاف أمرين في ذاتي أو عارض لا ينافي الاتفاق في الحقيقة الجنسية أو النوعية . قوله: (لمزيد الاستظهار) فالقدح فيه مع كونه إبطالاً للسند الأخص لا يقدح إلا في الاستظهار. قوله : ( لأنا) نقول يكفينا في مقام المنع الاختلاف بالهوية لا يقال : الاختلاف بالهوية حاصل بين العلمين المتعلقين بشيء واحد إذا كانا قائمين بمحلين فإن العلم القائم بزيد مخالف للعلم القائم بعمرو بالهوية مع أن التفاوت بينهما ليس كتفاوت إبصار زيد والعلم به، فعلم أن اختلافهما اختلاف قوى ليس مجرد الاختلاف بالهوية لأنا نقول : جاز أن تكون تلك الزيادة في الاختلاف بعوارض كلية صنفية، فلا يخرج بذلك من الاختلاف بالهوية إلى الاختلاف بالنوع القادح في المقصود. قوله : ( لمزيد الاستظهار يعني فغاية ما لزم مما ذكر عدم حصول هذا الغرض أعني مزيد الاستظهار ولا يقدح هذا في أصل الجواب، كما أن إبطال السند لا يضر المانع والأقرب أن يقال عن المصنف : إِنَّ ذلك الفرق الوجداني لا يمنع شيئاً من الاختلافين بل يجامع كلا منهما فلا يكون
The fifth section: Perceptions of the external senses. (The perceptions of the five external senses according to Shaykh al-Ash'ari are knowledge of their objects. So hearing, i.e., perception by the auditory sense, is knowledge of sounds; and sight, i.e., perception by the visual sense, is knowledge of visible things; and similarly for perception by touch, taste, and smell. So these senses are means to those knowledges acquired by their use, like innateness, intuition, and reflection which are used as means to the knowledges based on them. But the majority of theologians disagreed with him. For if we know something, like color for example, perfectly, then see it, we necessarily find a difference between the two states, and we know that the second state is different from the first without doubt. If sight were knowledge of the visible, there would be no difference. Likewise we find a difference between knowledge of this sound and hearing it, and between knowledge of this taste and tasting it, and between knowledge of this smell and smelling it. And he—i.e., al-Shaykh—can answer that that experiential difference does not prevent it—i.e., sensory perception—from being knowledge differing from other knowledges that are not based on senses, differing either by type or by identity. On the first view, knowledge would be a generic reality comprising different realities including sensory perception; on the second view, it would be a specific reality covering individuals differing in identity. It cannot be said: The dispute is about whether the reality of perceiving something by a sense is the reality of perceiving it commonly called knowledge or not. If we assume they differ by type, the debate becomes verbal, reducing to whether the word 'knowledge' is a name for absolute perception or for a type of it. For we say: It suffices for us in the context of denial the difference in identity, because the difference can be attributed to it; mentioning the difference in type is for greater corroboration. Also, his argument—i.e., the opponent's, namely the majority—is only valid if knowledge of the object were possible. His saying: (like innateness and intuition, etc.) means that just as these three are paths and means, so too the senses are paths, and the disclosure in all is only to the soul, so considering what is obtained by those paths as knowledge but not what is obtained by the senses is arbitrary. His saying: (And al-Shaykh can answer, etc.) Its summary is that the difference of two things in an essential or accidental attribute does not contradict agreement in generic or specific reality. His saying: (for greater corroboration), so finding fault with it, although it is an invalidation of the more specific proof, does not invalidate except the corroboration. His saying: (For we say: It suffices us in the context of denial the difference in identity.) It cannot be said: The difference in identity occurs between two knowledges related to one thing if they subsist in two loci, for the knowledge subsisting in Zayd differs from the knowledge subsisting in Amr in identity, yet the disparity between them is not like the disparity between Zayd's sight and knowledge of it. So it is known that their difference is a difference of faculties, not merely difference in identity. For we say: It is possible that that additional difference is due to universal specific accidents, so it does not thereby exit from difference in identity to difference in type that would damage the purpose. His saying: (for greater corroboration) means that the utmost consequence of what was mentioned is the non-attainment of this purpose, namely greater corroboration, and this does not damage the original answer, just as invalidating the proof does not harm the preventer. And it is more appropriate to say about the author: Indeed that experiential difference does not prevent either of the two differences, rather it coexists with each of them, so it is not...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of sensory perceptions as knowledge according to Ash'ari, followed by the majority objection and the Ash'ari response. The author states that for Ash'ari, perceptions of the five external senses are knowledge of their objects, with each sense corresponding to a specific type of object (hearing sounds, sight visible things, etc.). The senses are means to obtain knowledge, similar to innate knowledge (الوجدان), intuition (البديهة), and reflection (النظر). The majority of theologians object, arguing that there is a necessary experiential difference between perfect knowledge of an object (e.g., color) and actually seeing it, and similarly for other senses. If seeing were knowledge, there would be no such difference. The Ash'ari response, attributed to al-Shaykh, argues that this experiential difference does not prevent sensory perception from being knowledge; it may differ from other knowledge either in type (النوع) or in identity (الهوية). If by type, knowledge is a generic category with different species; if by identity, it is a species with different individuals. The objection that this reduces the debate to a verbal dispute is answered by saying that difference in identity suffices for denial, and mentioning type is only for stronger support. The commentator then elaborates: the difference could be due to accidental attributes without exiting the category of identity difference. The passage ends with the suggestion that the experiential difference does not negate either type of difference but coexists with both.

Key Terms
إدراك الحواس
علم
إبصار
سمع
وجدان
بديهة
اختيالف بالنوع
اختيالف بالهوية
حقيقة جنسية
حقيقة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position is often misunderstood as claiming sensory perception is identical to other knowledge, whereas it allows for difference by type or identity.
Correction: Sensory perception is still knowledge, but it differs from other knowledge either in type (species) or in individual identity.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, how can sensory perception be considered knowledge despite the felt difference from pure knowledge?
A) The difference is only in the object known
B) The difference is either by type or by identity
C) The difference is an illusion
D) The difference proves it is not knowledge

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view that sensory perceptions are knowledge is defended by allowing that they differ from other knowledge either in type or identity, thus preserving the concept of knowledge while acknowledging experiential distinctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive something through your senses, do you automatically accept it as certain knowledge, or do you sometimes doubt it? How does this lesson's claim that sensory perception is knowledge challenge or affirm your attitude?
?
Think of a time when you knew a fact intellectually but then experienced it through your senses—like the taste of a fruit you had only read about. Did the sensory experience feel like a different kind of knowledge? How does the Ash'ari explanation of difference by type or identity help you make sense of that experience?
?
If sensory perceptions are knowledge, then every sight, sound, and touch is a direct encounter with reality created by Allah. Are you living as if your senses are gifts that bring you knowledge of His signs, or do you take them for granted? What will you change today to treat your senses as sources of divine knowledge?

The Objection That the Dispute Becomes Verbal and the Initial Reply
Taftazani states the objection about the dispute becoming verbal and gives two replies: identity difference suffices, and senses are necessary for particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that the dispute becomes verbal is answered by appealing to identity difference, and why the argument from the impossibility of knowing particulars without senses is used.

Core Idea
Identity difference provides a substantive basis for distinguishing sense perception from knowledge, even without type difference.

The objector argues that if sense perception and other knowledge differ only in type, the dispute reduces to verbal definitions. Taftazani's reply shifts the focus: even difference in identity (huwiyya) is enough to explain why one thing is not the other. He then adds a second argument: the opponent's position would require knowing particulars without senses, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that the dispute becomes verbal if the type difference is assumed. Notice that the objector frames it as a question of whether 'knowledge' is a name for absolute perception or a type of it.
This sets up the challenge.
2
Identify the two parts of the reply
Taftazani gives two separate points: first, difference in identity suffices; second, the opponent's argument would require a way to know particulars without senses.
The first point is the main reply; the second is a supplementary argument.
3
Understand why identity difference suffices
Reflect on why difference in identity (al-ikhtilāf bi-l-huwiyya) is enough to explain the felt distinction between sense perception and knowledge, even if they are not different in type.
This is a key move in the debate.
4
Analyze the argument about particulars
Taftazani argues that sense perception is necessary for knowing particulars as particulars; therefore, the opponent cannot claim that knowledge of the same object can be obtained through another path.
This undermines the opponent's assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : الخلاف إنما هو في أن حقيقة إدراك الشيء بإحدى الحواس هل هي حقيقة إدراكه المسمى بالعلم اتفاقا ،أولا ، وإذا فرض اختلافهما بالنوع صار النزاع لفظيا راجعا إلى أن لفظ العلم اسم لمطلق الإدراك أو لنوع منه لأنا نقول : يكفينا في مقام المنع الاختلاف بالهوية لجواز استناد الفرق إليه، وذكر الاختلاف النوعي لمزيد الاستظهار. وأيضا فإنما يصح استدلاله أي استدلال الخصم أعني الجمهور لو أمكن العلم بمتعلقه بطريق آخر غير الحس، وهو باطل لأن الحس لا يتعلق إلا بالجزئيات من حيث خصوصياتها، ولا سبيل إلى إدراكها من هذه الجهة سوى الحس. فإن قلت : نحن نعلم أن في الجسم الفلاني مثلا لونا جزئيا مخصوصا علما تاما ثم ندركه بالبصر فنجد تفاوتا ضروريا، فقد صح إمكان أن يتعلق العلم بطريق آخر بما تعلق به الإدراك الحسي، قلت : هذا غلط، نشأ من عدم الفرق بين إدراك الجزئي على وجه جزئي، وبين إدراكه على وجه كلي وذلك لا يخفى على ذي مسكة. قوله: (لمزيد الاستظهار) فالقدح فيه مع كونه إبطالا للسند الأخص لا يقدح إلا في الاستظهار. قوله: ( لأنا) نقول يكفينا في مقام المنع الاختلاف بالهوية لا يقال : الاختلاف بالهوية حاصل بين العلمين المتعلقين بشيء واحد إذا كانا قائمين بمحلين فإن العلم القائم بزيد مخالف للعلم القائم بعمرو بالهوية مع أن التفاوت بينهما ليس كتفاوت إبصار زيد والعلم به، فعلم أن اختلافهما اختلاف قوى ليس مجرد الاختلاف بالهوية لأنا نقول : جاز أن تكون تلك الزيادة في الاختلاف بعوارض كلية صنفية، فلا يخرج بذلك من الاختلاف بالهوية إلى الاختلاف بالنوع القادح في المقصود.
It should not be said: The dispute is only about whether the reality of perceiving a thing by one of the senses is the reality of that perception called 'knowledge' by agreement or not, and if they are assumed to differ in type, the dispute becomes verbal, reducible to whether the word 'knowledge' is a name for absolute perception or for a type of it. For we say: In the context of denial, the difference in identity (al-ikhtilāf bi-l-huwiyya) suffices for us, because the difference can be based on it, and the mention of the difference in type is for additional support. Also, his argument, i.e., the argument of the opponent, the majority, would only be valid if knowledge of its object were possible by another way besides sense, but that is false because sense pertains only to particulars (al-juz'iyyāt) in terms of their particularities, and there is no way to perceive them from this aspect except sense. If you say: We know that in such and such body, for example, there is a particular color with complete knowledge, then we perceive it by sight and find a necessary difference, so it is established that knowledge can relate by another way to what sense perception relates to. I say: This is a mistake, arising from the failure to distinguish between perceiving the particular as particular and perceiving it as universal, and that is not hidden from anyone with discernment. His statement: (for additional support) so finding fault with it, while being an invalidation of the most specific proof, only harms the additional support. His statement: (for we say it suffices in the context of denial the difference in identity) it should not be said: The difference in identity occurs between two knowledges related to one thing when they subsist in two subjects, for the knowledge subsisting in Zayd is different from the knowledge subsisting in 'Amr in identity, yet the disparity between them is not like the disparity between Zayd's sight and knowledge of it. So it is known that their difference is a difference of powers, not merely a difference in identity. For we say: It is possible that the additional difference is due to general specific accidents (a'wāriḍ kulliyya ṣinfiyya), so it does not thereby go out from difference in identity to difference in type, which would damage the intended point.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The objector claims that if sense perceptions differ from other knowledge in type (naw'), then the dispute becomes merely verbal, since it reduces to whether 'knowledge' means absolute perception or a specific type. The answer is that even difference in identity (huwiyya) is sufficient to explain the felt distinction; mentioning type difference is only for additional support. The passage then presents a further argument: the opponent's proof would only be valid if it were possible to know the same object by another path besides sense. But this is false because sense alone grasps particulars in their particularity. If one objects that we can know a particular color completely and then see it, noticing a difference—implying knowledge can relate to the same object by another path—the reply is that this confuses perceiving the particular as particular (which is sensory) with perceiving it as universal (which is intellectual). The commentary then clarifies that even if difference in identity is accepted, the opponent might argue that identity difference between two knowledges of the same thing (like Zayd's and Amr's knowledge) is not like the difference between sight and knowledge of the same thing. The answer is that the additional difference can be due to general specific accidents, so it remains a difference in identity, not type, and thus does not undermine the response.

Key Terms
نزاع لفظي
اختلاف بالهوية
اختلاف بالنوع
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's reply, why does the difference in identity suffice even if the dispute becomes verbal on the assumption of difference in type?
A) Because identity difference makes the felt distinction real and does not reduce to mere terminology.
B) Because the opponent concedes the dispute is verbal.
C) Because type difference is impossible.
D) Because both types are identical in essence.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani defends the substantive nature of the dispute by showing that identity difference is sufficient to distinguish sense perception from knowledge, and that sense perception uniquely grasps particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a disagreement, do you ever reduce it to a 'mere verbal dispute' to avoid facing the real difference? How might that be a form of intellectual laziness? Commit to examining one disagreement this week where you might be hiding behind semantics.
?
Taftazani insists that even a difference in identity (not just type) is enough to make a dispute real. In your own life, do you sometimes dismiss small differences as insignificant when they actually matter? What would change if you took every identity-level difference seriously? Name one relationship where you have overlooked such a difference.

Further Objections: Knowing Particulars and the Nature of Identity Difference
Taftazani responds to two further objections: one about knowing particulars by other means, and another about identity difference between knowledges in different subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the further objections regarding knowing particulars and the nature of identity difference, and how Taftazani defends his position.

Core Idea
Perceiving a particular as particular is sensory; perceiving it as universal is intellectual; and identity difference remains substantive even when additional accidents are present.

The objector raises a counterexample: we can know a particular color intellectually and then perceive it by sight, noticing a difference. Taftazani corrects this by distinguishing between perceiving the particular as particular (sensory) and as universal (intellectual). The objector also challenges the sufficiency of identity difference, arguing that identity difference between knowledges in different subjects is unlike that between sense and knowledge. Taftazani replies that additional differences can arise from general specific accidents without changing the identity difference.

Guided Path
1
Follow the second objection about knowing a particular color
Read the objection: we can have complete knowledge of a particular color and then sense it, noticing a difference. This seems to show that knowledge can relate to the same object through another path.
This is a concrete example to test the theory.
2
Understand the distinction between particular and universal
Taftazani's reply hinges on the idea that intellectual knowledge of a particular is always universal, not particular as particular. Only sense grasps the particular in its particularity.
This distinction is crucial for the entire debate.
3
Examine the objection about identity difference in different subjects
The objector points out that identity difference between Zayd's knowledge and Amr's knowledge is not like the difference between Zayd's sight and his knowledge. So there must be more than just identity difference.
The objector tries to show that identity difference alone is insufficient.
4
See how general specific accidents preserve identity difference
Taftazani explains that the extra difference can be due to 'general specific accidents' (a'wāriḍ kulliyya ṣinfiyya) which do not change the fundamental identity difference.
This allows the argument to stand.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that having complete knowledge of a particular color and then sensing it shows that the same object can be known both intellectually and sensorily as a particular.

?

?

?

Rational Forms: Non-Mutual Exclusivity in Inherence
Taftazani enumerates the first way rational forms differ from external forms: non-mutual exclusivity in inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first distinction between rational and external forms: rational forms can coexist in one subject without mutual exclusivity.

Core Idea
Rational forms can inhere together; external forms cannot.

Taftazani begins the philosophers' discussion of rational forms by highlighting a key difference from external forms. Though they share the same essence, rational forms are not mutually exclusive in inherence. This sets the stage for a deeper understanding of the nature of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Taftazani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the first distinction
The passage states that rational forms are 'not mutually exclusive in inherence'. Contrast this with external forms, which are mutually exclusive.
The example given is a shape that cannot take another shape simultaneously in the external world.
3
Understand the commentary's clarifications
The commentary explains that 'rational forms' includes both particular and universal mental concepts. The equality in essence is what makes the distinction noteworthy.
Even if forms differ in truth-value (like negation and affirmation), they can coexist in the mind.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: What does this tell us about the nature of mental existence compared to physical existence? How does this affect our understanding of knowledge?
This distinction is foundational for the philosophers' theory of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية فيما يتفرع على القول بثبوت الصورة العقلية الحكماء قالوا : الصورة العقلية تمتاز عن الخارجية مع التساوي في نفس الماهية بوجوه. الأول: أنها أي الصور العقلية غير متمانعة في الحلول إذ يجوز حلولها معا في محل واحد بخلاف الصور الخارجية، فإن المتشكل بشكل مخصوص مثلا لا يمتنع أن يتشكل بشكل آخر مع. قوله: ( بثبوت الصور العقلية أي الحاصلة عند العقل جزئية كانت أو كلية. قوله: (مع التساوي إلخ إنما قيد بذلك لأنه المحتاج إلى البيان فإن المتخالفين في الحقيقة متخالفان في اللوازم والأحكام. قوله: ( في الحلول ) وإن كانت متخالفة في الصدق كالنفي والإثبات. قوله: ( بخلاف الصور الخارجية جوهرية أو عرضية ولذا مثل بالمثالين.
The sixth purpose: the saying of the philosophers concerning the rational forms, regarding what branches off from the affirmation of the rational form. The philosophers said: The rational form is distinguished from the external form, while being equal in the essence itself, in several ways. The first: that they, i.e., the rational forms, are not mutually exclusive in inherence (al-ḥulūl), for it is permissible for them to inhere together in one subject, unlike external forms. For a thing that is formed with a particular shape, for example, is not prevented from being formed with another shape together. His statement: (on the affirmation of rational forms) i.e., what is obtained in the intellect, whether particular or universal. His statement: (while being equal etc.) is only qualified by that because it is what needs explanation, for things that differ in reality differ in their consequences and rulings. His statement: (in inherence) even if they differ in truth-value like negation and affirmation. His statement: (unlike external forms) substantial or accidental, and therefore he gave the two examples.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth purpose, which is the philosophers' doctrine on rational forms. It explains that rational forms are distinguished from external forms in several aspects despite equality in essence (mahiyya). The first aspect is non-mutual exclusivity in inherence (al-ḥulūl): multiple rational forms can coexist in a single subject, whereas external forms cannot. For example, a body with a particular shape cannot simultaneously have another shape. The commentary clarifies that 'rational forms' refers to what is acquired in the intellect, whether particular or universal. The qualification 'while being equal in essence' is necessary because if they differed in reality, they would already differ in consequences; here they share essence but differ in other respects. The comment on 'in inherence' notes that rational forms can coexist even if they differ in truth-value (like affirmation and negation). Finally, external forms can be either substantial or accidental, and the example (the two examples) illustrates that.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية
الصورة الخارجية
الحلول
التمانع
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational forms are identical to external forms because they share the same essence.
Correction: They share essence but differ in properties like inherence: rational forms can coexist, external forms cannot.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect by which rational forms are distinguished from external forms?
A) They are only universal, not particular.
B) They can inhere together in one subject without mutual exclusivity.
C) They are not real but only mental.
D) They are always true, unlike external forms which can be false.

Key Takeaway
Rational forms are not mutually exclusive in inherence, allowing multiple forms to inhabit the same subject, unlike external forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your mental concepts as if they were physical objects, expecting them to be mutually exclusive? When you caught yourself, how did that affect your understanding of a complex idea like the attributes of Allah? This week, consciously remind yourself that multiple truths can coexist in your mind without contradiction.
?
The philosophers distinguish between mental and external existence. In your own life, do you confuse your mental image of a person with their actual external reality? Think of one specific relationship where you have done this. What would change if you consistently separated the two? Commit to asking yourself before judging someone: Am I reacting to my mental form or the external form?

First Distinction: Intellectual Forms Can Coexist
Al-Jurjani explains coexistence of intellectual forms and growth of preparedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intellectual forms can coexist in the soul without mutual hindrance, unlike physical forms, and that prior knowledge increases the soul's preparedness.

Core Idea
Intellectual forms harmonize where physical forms clash.

This passage presents the first of four key distinctions between intellectual and external forms. Al-Jurjani argues that multiple intellectual forms can reside together in the soul without conflict, a capacity that physical forms lack. Moreover, the soul's ability to receive new forms grows with prior learning. This insight lays the foundation for understanding how knowledge accumulates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author contrasts intellectual forms with external forms.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that intellectual forms 'are not mutually preventing in indwelling.' Ask yourself why this matters for how we understand the soul's capacity for knowledge.
This is the core of the first distinction.
3
Consider the examples
The passage gives examples of shape and fiery form. Reflect on how these illustrate the difference between intellectual and physical forms.
Physical forms cannot coexist; intellectual forms can.
4
Reflect on preparedness
The last sentence describes how the soul's preparedness increases with prior sciences. Think about how this relates to your own learning experience.
This connects to the broader idea of cumulative knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أنها أي الصور العقلية غير متمانعة في الحلول إذ يجوز حلولها معاً في محل واحد بخلاف الصور الخارجية، فإن المتشكل بشكل مخصوص مثلاً لا يمتنع أن يتشكل بشكل آخر مع الشكل الأول، وكذا المادة المتصورة بالصورة النارية يستحيل أن تتصور معها بصورة أخرى. بل الصور العقلية متفاوتة في الحلول، فإن النفس إذا كانت خالية عن العلوم كان تصورها لشيء من الحقائق عسيراً جداً، وإذا اتصفت ببعض العلوم زاد استعدادها للباقي وسهل انتقاشها به.
First: They, that is the intellectual forms, are not mutually preventing in indwelling, for it is permissible for them to indwell together in one locus, unlike external forms. For example, something that is shaped with a particular shape does not preclude taking on another shape along with the first shape. Likewise, matter that is conceived with a fiery form cannot be conceived with another form along with it. Rather, intellectual forms are varied in indwelling, for when the soul is empty of sciences, its conception of something from realities is very difficult, and when it becomes qualified with some sciences, its preparedness for the rest increases and its impression by them becomes easy.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four distinctions between intellectual forms and external forms. It emphasizes that multiple intellectual forms can coexist in the same locus (the soul) without mutual hindrance, unlike physical forms such as a shape or fiery form which cannot combine. The passage also notes that the soul's capacity to receive intellectual forms increases with prior knowledge, making it easier to acquire new ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think intellectual forms are like physical forms, mutually exclusive in the soul.
Correction: Intellectual forms can coexist in the soul without conflict, as Al-Jurjani explains.

Checkpoint
According to the first distinction, which of the following is true about intellectual forms?
A) They cannot exist together in the same soul.
B) They are mutually preventing like physical forms.
C) They can coexist in one locus without conflict.
D) They are easier to acquire when the soul is empty of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual forms can coexist in the soul, and prior knowledge enhances the soul's readiness to receive new forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find learning a new Islamic science difficult, do you attribute it to the subject's depth or to your own lack of prior preparation? What will you do today to increase your preparedness for that science?
?
Your soul can hold many truths at once—are you willing to let go of any false beliefs that conflict with what you have learned? Which belief will you examine this week?
?
The empty soul struggles to conceive realities. Are you feeding your soul with knowledge or leaving it empty? Commit to one specific text or lesson you will study this week to fill your soul.

Second Distinction: Large Forms with Small Forms
Al-Jurjani states the second distinction regarding coexistence of large and small intellectual forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second distinction that large intellectual forms can coexist with small ones in the same locus, and why this differs from material forms.

Core Idea
The soul can hold vast and tiny images together, unlike physical space.

Al-Jurjani presents the second key distinction between intellectual and material forms. While physical objects cannot occupy the same space if they differ greatly in size, the soul has the remarkable capacity to imagine enormous and tiny things simultaneously. This passage explains that capacity and what it reveals about the nature of intellectual forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast between intellectual and material forms.
Pay attention to the examples given.
2
Identify the distinction
The passage states that large and small intellectual forms can indwell together. Identify the examples (heavens, earth, mountains, small things).
Contrast with material forms.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinguishes intellectual forms from material ones. What does this tell us about the soul's capacity?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: تحل الكبيرة من الصور العقلية في محل الصغيرة منها معا، ولذلك تقدر النفس على تخيل السموات والأرض والجبال والأمور الصغيرة بالمرة معاً، بخلاف الصور المادية فإن العظيمة منها لا تحل في محل الصغيرة مجتمعة معها.
Second: The large (intellectual form) indwells in the locus of the small (form) together with it. Therefore, the soul is able to imagine the heavens, earth, mountains, and very small things all together, unlike material forms, for the large among them does not indwell in the locus of the small together with it.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the second distinction: the soul can hold both large and small intellectual forms simultaneously, such as imagining the heavens and a tiny grain. In contrast, material forms cannot occupy the same place due to physical size constraints. This highlights the non-spatial nature of intellectual forms.

Key Takeaway

The Third Distinction: Weak Not Obliterated by Strong
Al-Jurjani states the third distinction and explains it with a comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a weak intellectual form persists in the perceiving power despite the arrival of a stronger form, and how this differs from physical matter.

Core Idea
In the intellect, weak and strong forms coexist; in matter, the weaker is obliterated.

Al-Jurjani now presents the third distinction between intellectual forms and physical qualities. Unlike the external world where a stronger quality overpowers and erases a weaker one, the perceiving power can retain both simultaneously. This brief passage highlights the non-physical nature of intellectual forms and their independence from material constraints.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style of Al-Jurjani.
2
Identify the rule
The passage begins with a rule: 'The weak is not obliterated by the strong.' Underline this key claim.
3
Understand the intellectual vs. external contrast
Al-Jurjani explains that the rule applies to intellectual forms but not to external matter. Mark the contrasting phrase 'بخلاف الخارجية'.
Consider why this difference matters for the nature of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this distinction tell us about the nature of the perceiving power? It shows that intellectual forms are not subject to physical competition.
This supports the idea that the intellect is immaterial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: لا ينمحي الضعيف بالقوي، يعني أن الصورة العقلية للكيفية الضعيفة لا تزول عن القوة المدركة بسبب حصول صورة الكيفية القوية فيها، بخلاف الخارجية، فإن الكيفية الضعيفة منها تنمحي عن المادة عند حصول الكيفية القوية فيها.
Third: The weak is not obliterated by the strong, meaning that the intellectual form of a weak quality does not vanish from the perceiving power due to the occurrence of the form of a strong quality in it, unlike the external, for the weak quality among them is obliterated from matter when the strong quality occurs in it.

Commentary
This passage presents the third distinction: in the intellect, a weak idea or image is not erased when a stronger one comes, unlike physical objects where a stronger quality (e.g., heat) overpowers and removes a weaker one (e.g., warmth) from matter. This demonstrates the non-physical nature of intellectual forms.

Fourth Distinction: Easy Retrieval of Intellectual Forms
Al-Jurjani argues that intellectual forms persist and are easily recalled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intellectual forms are not inherently transient and can be recalled without new acquisition, because the soul is eternal.

Core Idea
Intellectual forms are lasting; forgetting is not true erasure but temporary loss easily recovered.

Al-Jurjani continues his distinctions by addressing what happens to the intellectual forms that the soul acquires. While sense perceptions may fade, intellectual images behave differently. He argues they do not necessarily vanish, and even if forgotten, they can be recalled without starting from scratch. This distinction rests on the nature of the soul as eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the twofold claim: (1) intellectual forms do not necessarily vanish, and (2) if they vanish, retrieval is easy.
The passage includes the matn and a brief commentary.
2
Identify the reason
The commentary states that the soul is eternal by agreement, which explains why the forms do not necessarily vanish. Understand that this is a foundational premise.
The term 'by agreement' refers to a widely accepted view among theologians.
3
Consider the qualification
Note that ease of retrieval is not absolute. The commentary clarifies that it may be so, but not necessarily for every intellectual form. This prevents overgeneralization.
This nuance is important for accurate application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: الصورة العقلية إذا حصلت في العاقلة لا يجب زوالها، وإذا زالت سهل استرجاعها من غير حاجة إلى تجشم كسب جديد. قوله: ( لا يجب زوالها لأن النفس أبدي بالاتفاق. قوله: (سهل استرجاعها يعني أنه قد يكون كذلك لا أن كل صورة عقلية زالت كذلك.
Fourth: The intellectual form, when it occurs in the intellect, does not necessarily vanish, and if it vanishes, its retrieval is easy without the need to undergo a new acquisition. (Commentary: 'does not necessarily vanish' because the soul is eternal by agreement. 'Easy retrieval' means that it may be so, not that every intellectual form that vanishes is like that.)
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth distinction: intellectual forms are not inherently transient; they can persist in the soul. Even if forgotten, they can be recalled easily without starting from scratch. The commentary clarifies that this is because the soul is eternal, and the ease of retrieval is typical but not universal.

Universality as Matching (Mutabaqa)
Explains universality as matching with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an intellectual form is considered universal through the concept of matching (mutabaqa) rather than literal commonality.

Core Idea
Universality of intellectual forms is about matching, not sharing.

This passage explains what it means for an intellectual form to be universal. The author introduces the key term 'matching' (mutabaqa) and shows with examples that the same abstracted meaning arises from any individual of a species when stripped of its particularities.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mutabaqa
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on how the author defines 'matching' in relation to Zayd, Amr, and Bakr.
Note that the form is said to be 'exactly the same' impression from each individual.
2
Understand the example
Work through the example: when one individual (e.g., Zayd) is present in the imagination and stripped of individuators, the resulting form precludes a new impression from another individual like Amr. Why? Because the form is identical.
The contrast with a horse shows that a different species yields a different form.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that this matching is what makes the intellectual form universal and shared among many, not any literal common existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية ( كانت ) تلك الصورة كلية على معنى أنها تكون ( مطابقة لـزيـد وعمرو وبـكـر) إلى سائر أفراده والمراد بالمطابقة ما فسره بقوله : ( أي كل واحد ) من تلك الأفراد ( إذا ) حضر في الخيال و (جرد عن مشخصاته كانت تلك الصورة أعني صورة المعنى المشترك ( هي بعينها ( الأثر ( الحاصل منه ) ، أي من ذلك الواحد الذي جرد مشخصاته لا تختلف تلك الصورة باختلاف الأفراد التي تجرد عن المشخصات، حتى إذا سبق واحد منها إلى النفس فتأثرت منه بذلك الأثر المجرد عن العوارض لم يكن لما عداه من الأفراد إذا حضر عندها تأثير آخر، وإذا كان هذا المتأخر سابقاً انعكس الحال بينهما، ولو كان الحاضر من غير أفراده كفرس مثلاً لكان الأثر الحاصل في القوة العاقلة بالتجريد عن المشخصات صورة أخرى سوى صورة الإنسان، فهذا معنى كون الصورة العقلية كلية مشتركة بين كثيرين فإن قلت : لا شك أن الصورة العقلية الإنسانية الحالة في القوة العاقلة صورة جزئية معروضة لعوارض ذهنية باعتبار حلولها في نفس جزئية، ولذلك امتازت عن الصورة بمعنى الأمر الموجود في الذهن وجوداً ظلياً، ومنشأ الاعتراض عدم الفرق بين المعنيين للصورة، قال الشيخ في منطق الشفاء : اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون معناه الواحد الذي يدل عليه لا يمتنع في الذهن من حيث أنه تصور اشتراك الكثيرة فيه على السوية بأن يقال لكل واحد منها أنه هو اشتراكاً على درجة واحدة أو يمتنع، والأول يسمى كلياً والثاني جزئياً إلى أن قال: الكلي المعنى المفهوم في النفس لا يمتنع نسبته إلى أشياء كثيرة تطابقها نسبة متشاكلة فإن قلت : أول كلام الشيخ يدل على أن الاشتراك بمعنى الحمل وآخره يدل على أنه بمعنى المطابقة قلت : المطابقة المذكورة مالها الاتحاد بين الحاصل في الذهن وبين الماهية المجردة عن المشخصات وهو معنى الحمل؛ فمآل العبارتين واحد، إلا أن المطابقة تتضمن بيان كيفية الحمل بينهما، وأنه باعتبار الاتحاد في الماهية. فهذا معنى كون الصورة العقلية كلية وليس معناه الاشتراك حقيقة إما بطريق التشعب والتجزي فهو ظاهر، أو بوجودها في محال متعددة تتصف بصفات متقابلة فإنه باطل بديهة، وإن ذهب إليه القائلون بوجود الطبائع مصرين على أن شأن الأمور الكلية أن تتصف بالمتقابلات، وتوجد في المحال المتعددة، وهذا لا ينافي لتغاير موصوفهما باعتبار فإنها مع قطع عن المشخصات
The second type - the sixth topic: The statement of the philosophers regarding intellectual forms. That form is universal in the sense that it is matching to Zayd, Amr, and Bakr, up to all its individuals. And what is meant by matching is as he explained by his saying: that each one of those individuals, when it is present in the imagination and stripped of its individuators, that form—i.e., the form of the common meaning—is exactly the same impression obtained from it—i.e., from that one which is stripped of its individuators. That form does not differ with the difference of the individuals that are stripped of individuators. So if one of them precedes to the soul and it is affected by that impression stripped of accidents, there is no other effect from the other individuals when they are present. And if this later one was earlier, the situation is reversed. And if what is present were from other than its individuals, like a horse for example, the impression obtained in the rational power by stripping of individuators would be another form besides the form of man. So this is the meaning of the intellectual form being universal shared among many. If you say: There is no doubt that the human intellectual form existing in the rational power is a particular form subject to mental accidents by virtue of its inherence in a particular soul, and therefore it is distinguished from the form meaning the thing existing in the mind as a shadowy existence. And the origin of the objection is the failure to differentiate between the two meanings of form. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) said in his Logic of al-Shifa: The single word either has a meaning such that it is not impossible in the mind, in that it can be conceived as shared among many equally such that each of them is said to be it on equal degree, or it is impossible. The first is called universal, the second particular. And he said: The universal is the meaning conceived in the soul whose relation to many things that match it with a similar relation is not impossible. If you say: The first part of the Shaykh's words indicates that commonality is in the sense of predication, and the latter indicates that it is in the sense of matching. I say: The mentioned matching is the union between what is obtained in the mind and the essence stripped of individuators, and that is the meaning of predication; so the two expressions lead to the same end, except that matching includes an explanation of the manner of predication between them, and that it is by virtue of union in essence. So this is the meaning of the intellectual form being universal, and its meaning is not real commonality, either by way of ramification and division (that is apparent), or by its existence in multiple loci that are described by opposite attributes, for that is evidently false, even if those who affirm the existence of natures insist on that—that the nature of universal things is to be described by opposites and to exist in multiple loci, but this does not contradict the diversity of their subjects by virtue of abstraction from individuators.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the 'matching' (mutabaqa) that constitutes the universality of intellectual forms, followed by a response to an objection. It begins by stating that an intellectual form (e.g., humanity) is universal in the sense that it 'matches' each individual (like Zayd, Amr, Bakr) when that individual is stripped in the imagination of its individuators. The matching means that the form—the common meaning—produces the exact same mental impression from any individual, so that if one individual is present first, the soul's impression from it precludes a new impression from another individual of the same species. But if a different species (e.g., horse) is present, the impression is a different form. So the universality is not that the form is literally common (as a single entity in multiple places), but that it applies identically to all individuals when abstracted. The passage then raises an objection: the intellectual form 'humanity' in the mind is itself a particular form because it inheres in a particular soul, so how can it be universal? The answer distinguishes between the form as a mental accident (particular) and the form as the abstracted essence (matching). The author cites Ibn Sina's distinction between two definitions of universality: commonality in predication (ishtirak fi al-haml) and matching (mutabaqa). These are ultimately equivalent, but matching explains that the mind's form unites with the essence stripped of individuators, which is the basis for predication of the universal. The passage concludes by rejecting any real commonality of universals—whether by division of a single thing into parts or by existence in multiple subjects with opposite attributes—as false, even if some (like the upholders of the Platonic natures) claim otherwise. The true meaning of universality is this matching, not a literal common existence.

Key Terms
المطابقة
القوة العاقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a universal form is a single entity that exists in multiple places simultaneously.
Correction: The passage clarifies that universality means the same abstracted meaning matches each individual when stripped of individuators, not literal co-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a second individual of the same species is presented to the soul after the first?
A) A new and different impression is formed.
B) No new impression is formed because the form is identical.
C) The soul rejects the second individual.
D) The second individual produces a different form.

Key Takeaway
An intellectual form is universal because the same abstracted meaning matches each individual of a species, not because it is a shared entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at people around you, do you primarily see their individuators—their appearance, status, or background—or do you recognize the common human form that matches each of them? How does this affect the way you treat them?
?
If the intellectual form of humanity is the same for all individuals, then your judgment of others should be based on that common essence, not on superficial differences. Examine a recent situation where you judged someone based on their outward features. What would change if you had focused on the universal human form? Pledge to practice seeing the essence in every person you meet tomorrow.

Objection and Answer: How Can a Mental Form Be Universal?
Addresses objection and resolves by distinguishing two meanings of form and citing Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that an intellectual form is particular is resolved by distinguishing two meanings of 'form' and by reconciling Ibn Sina's definitions.

Core Idea
Universality is matching, not literal commonality; form-as-accident is particular, form-as-essence is universal.

An objection arises: the intellectual form in the mind is a particular accident, so how can it be universal? The author resolves by distinguishing two meanings of 'form' and citing Ibn Sina to show that commonality in predication is the same as matching.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the intellectual form exists in a particular soul and has mental accidents, so it is particular. The origin of the objection is failing to distinguish two meanings of form.
One meaning is the form as a mental accident; the other is the form as the abstracted essence.
2
Study Ibn Sina's definitions
Read Ibn Sina's two definitions of universality: one based on commonality in predication, the other on matching. Note how the author reconciles them.
The key is that matching means the union of the mental form with the essence stripped of individuators.
3
Rejection of real commonality
The author rejects any literal commonality of universals—whether by division or by existence in multiple loci with opposite attributes—as false.
This refutes the view of those who affirm Platonic natures.

Source Text
