النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية ( كانت ) تلك الصورة كلية على معنى أنها تكون ( مطابقة لـزيـد وعمرو وبـكـر) إلى سائر أفراده والمراد بالمطابقة ما فسره بقوله : ( أي كل واحد ) من تلك الأفراد ( إذا ) حضر في الخيال و (جرد عن مشخصاته كانت تلك الصورة أعني صورة المعنى المشترك ( هي بعينها ( الأثر ( الحاصل منه ) ، أي من ذلك الواحد الذي جرد مشخصاته لا تختلف تلك الصورة باختلاف الأفراد التي تجرد عن المشخصات، حتى إذا سبق واحد منها إلى النفس فتأثرت منه بذلك الأثر المجرد عن العوارض لم يكن لما عداه من الأفراد إذا حضر عندها تأثير آخر، وإذا كان هذا المتأخر سابقاً انعكس الحال بينهما، ولو كان الحاضر من غير أفراده كفرس مثلاً لكان الأثر الحاصل في القوة العاقلة بالتجريد عن المشخصات صورة أخرى سوى صورة الإنسان، فهذا معنى كون الصورة العقلية كلية مشتركة بين كثيرين فإن قلت : لا شك أن الصورة العقلية الإنسانية الحالة في القوة العاقلة صورة جزئية معروضة لعوارض ذهنية باعتبار حلولها في نفس جزئية، ولذلك امتازت عن الصورة بمعنى الأمر الموجود في الذهن وجوداً ظلياً، ومنشأ الاعتراض عدم الفرق بين المعنيين للصورة، قال الشيخ في منطق الشفاء : اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون معناه الواحد الذي يدل عليه لا يمتنع في الذهن من حيث أنه تصور اشتراك الكثيرة فيه على السوية بأن يقال لكل واحد منها أنه هو اشتراكاً على درجة واحدة أو يمتنع، والأول يسمى كلياً والثاني جزئياً إلى أن قال: الكلي المعنى المفهوم في النفس لا يمتنع نسبته إلى أشياء كثيرة تطابقها نسبة متشاكلة فإن قلت : أول كلام الشيخ يدل على أن الاشتراك بمعنى الحمل وآخره يدل على أنه بمعنى المطابقة قلت : المطابقة المذكورة مالها الاتحاد بين الحاصل في الذهن وبين الماهية المجردة عن المشخصات وهو معنى الحمل؛ فمآل العبارتين واحد، إلا أن المطابقة تتضمن بيان كيفية الحمل بينهما، وأنه باعتبار الاتحاد في الماهية. فهذا معنى كون الصورة العقلية كلية وليس معناه الاشتراك حقيقة إما بطريق التشعب والتجزي فهو ظاهر، أو بوجودها في محال متعددة تتصف بصفات متقابلة فإنه باطل بديهة، وإن ذهب إليه القائلون بوجود الطبائع مصرين على أن شأن الأمور الكلية أن تتصف بالمتقابلات، وتوجد في المحال المتعددة، وهذا لا ينافي لتغاير موصوفهما باعتبار فإنها مع قطع عن المشخصات
The second type - the sixth topic: The statement of the philosophers regarding intellectual forms. That form is universal in the sense that it is matching to Zayd, Amr, and Bakr, up to all its individuals. And what is meant by matching is as he explained by his saying: that each one of those individuals, when it is present in the imagination and stripped of its individuators, that form—i.e., the form of the common meaning—is exactly the same impression obtained from it—i.e., from that one which is stripped of its individuators. That form does not differ with the difference of the individuals that are stripped of individuators. So if one of them precedes to the soul and it is affected by that impression stripped of accidents, there is no other effect from the other individuals when they are present. And if this later one was earlier, the situation is reversed. And if what is present were from other than its individuals, like a horse for example, the impression obtained in the rational power by stripping of individuators would be another form besides the form of man. So this is the meaning of the intellectual form being universal shared among many. If you say: There is no doubt that the human intellectual form existing in the rational power is a particular form subject to mental accidents by virtue of its inherence in a particular soul, and therefore it is distinguished from the form meaning the thing existing in the mind as a shadowy existence. And the origin of the objection is the failure to differentiate between the two meanings of form. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) said in his Logic of al-Shifa: The single word either has a meaning such that it is not impossible in the mind, in that it can be conceived as shared among many equally such that each of them is said to be it on equal degree, or it is impossible. The first is called universal, the second particular. And he said: The universal is the meaning conceived in the soul whose relation to many things that match it with a similar relation is not impossible. If you say: The first part of the Shaykh's words indicates that commonality is in the sense of predication, and the latter indicates that it is in the sense of matching. I say: The mentioned matching is the union between what is obtained in the mind and the essence stripped of individuators, and that is the meaning of predication; so the two expressions lead to the same end, except that matching includes an explanation of the manner of predication between them, and that it is by virtue of union in essence. So this is the meaning of the intellectual form being universal, and its meaning is not real commonality, either by way of ramification and division (that is apparent), or by its existence in multiple loci that are described by opposite attributes, for that is evidently false, even if those who affirm the existence of natures insist on that—that the nature of universal things is to be described by opposites and to exist in multiple loci, but this does not contradict the diversity of their subjects by virtue of abstraction from individuators.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the 'matching' (mutabaqa) that constitutes the universality of intellectual forms, followed by a response to an objection. It begins by stating that an intellectual form (e.g., humanity) is universal in the sense that it 'matches' each individual (like Zayd, Amr, Bakr) when that individual is stripped in the imagination of its individuators. The matching means that the form—the common meaning—produces the exact same mental impression from any individual, so that if one individual is present first, the soul's impression from it precludes a new impression from another individual of the same species. But if a different species (e.g., horse) is present, the impression is a different form. So the universality is not that the form is literally common (as a single entity in multiple places), but that it applies identically to all individuals when abstracted. The passage then raises an objection: the intellectual form 'humanity' in the mind is itself a particular form because it inheres in a particular soul, so how can it be universal? The answer distinguishes between the form as a mental accident (particular) and the form as the abstracted essence (matching). The author cites Ibn Sina's distinction between two definitions of universality: commonality in predication (ishtirak fi al-haml) and matching (mutabaqa). These are ultimately equivalent, but matching explains that the mind's form unites with the essence stripped of individuators, which is the basis for predication of the universal. The passage concludes by rejecting any real commonality of universals—whether by division of a single thing into parts or by existence in multiple subjects with opposite attributes—as false, even if some (like the upholders of the Platonic natures) claim otherwise. The true meaning of universality is this matching, not a literal common existence.

الاشتراك
الحمل
الماهية المجردة
الطبائع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the universal form in the mind is a single entity that exists in many minds.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the universality lies in the matching relation, not in the form itself being literally common.

Checkpoint
How does the author reconcile Ibn Sina's two definitions of universality?
A) He says they are contradictory and one must be rejected.
B) He explains that matching is the union between the mental form and the essence stripped of individuators, which is the meaning of predication.
C) He says the first definition applies to words, the second to concepts.
D) He ignores the first definition and adopts only the second.

Key Takeaway
The universality of an intellectual form is not literal commonality but the matching relation between the mental form and the abstracted essence, which is the basis of predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the concept 'human', do you tend to imagine a real 'human nature' floating somewhere? How does this lesson challenge you to refine your understanding of what your mind actually does when it forms universal ideas?
?
If universality is only a matching relation and not real commonality, what does that mean for your reliance on concepts like 'justice', 'truth', or 'goodness' in your daily judgments? Are they merely mental matches, or do they have a deeper grounding?
?
This passage insists that universal concepts are not real entities existing in multiple places. How might this affect the way you think about Allah's attributes—especially His knowledge—which encompasses all universals without multiplicity? Does this make His knowledge more sublime or more abstract to you?

Three States of Knowledge and al-Razi's Objection
Sanusi enumerates three states of knowledge and gives an analogy, then cites al-Razi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three psychological states involved in producing an answer to a question (ignorance, simple awareness, detailed knowledge) and the intuitive argument for them, as well as al-Razi's objection to the concept of total knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge progresses from ignorance to simple awareness to detailed understanding.

Sanusi presents a phenomenological analysis of how we come to know an answer after being asked a question. He identifies three distinct states — ignorance, a simple beginning of knowledge, and detailed awareness — and provides an analogy from vision. He then introduces al-Razi's objection to total knowledge, which challenges the coherence of the simple state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Observe the sequence of states described.
The text uses technical terms like 'مبدأ التفاصيل' and 'القوة المحضة'.
2
Identify the three states
Find the three states: ignorance (حالة الجهل), the simple state after the question (أمر بسيط), and the detailed state (الحالة التفصيلية). Note how they differ.
The simple state is 'مبدأ التفاصيل' — a beginning of details, not yet fully actualized.
3
Understand the analogy
Read the analogy of seeing livestock all at once versus one by one. Relate total vision to total knowledge and detailed vision to detailed knowledge.
The analogy shows that total and detailed are both real modes of perception.
4
Analyze al-Razi's objection
Read al-Razi's objection that a single image cannot correspond to many different things. Note that his argument is incomplete in this passage.
This objection challenges the very possibility of total knowledge. The answer is not provided here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يأخذ في تقريره أي تقرير الجواب ) فيلاحظ تفصيله ) بملاحظة أجزائه واحداً بعد واحد ففي ذهنه حال ما سئل (أمر بسيط وهو مبدأ التفاصيل) الحاصلة في ثاني الحال ) والتفرقة بين تلك الحالة ) الحاصلة دفعة عقيب السؤال ( وبين حالة الجهل ) الثابتة قبل السؤال ) وملاحظة التفصيل) المتفرعة على التقرير ( ضرورية ) وجدانية إذ في حالة الجهل المسماة عقلاً بالفعل ليس إدراك الجواب حاصلاً بالفعل، بل النفس في تلك الحالة تقوى على اسحضاره وتفصيله بلا تجشم كسب جديد، فهناك قوة محضة وفي الحالة الحاصلة عقيب السؤال قد حصل بالفعل شعور وعلم ما بالجواب لم يكن حاصلاً ،قبله وفي الحالة التفصيلية صارت الأجزاء ملحوظة قصداً، ولم يكن ذلك حاصلاً في شيء من الحالتين السابقتين ( وشبه ذلك بمن يرى نعماً) كثيراً ( تارة دفعة فإنه يرى في هذه الحالة ( جميع أجزائه ) أي أجزاء ذلك النعم ( ضرورة وتارة بأن يحدق البصر نحو واحد واحد فيميزه أي النعم ويفصل أجزاءه بعضها عن بعض، فالرؤية الأولى رؤية إجمالية، والثانية رؤية تفصيلية والفرق بينهما معلوم بالوجدان فقس حالة البصيرة بالنسبة إلى مدركاتها على حال البصر بالقياس إلى مدركاته في ثبوت مثل هاتين الحالتين فيها أيضاً ) قال الإمام الرازي : ) في إنكار العلم الإجمالي ( يمتنع حصول صورة واحدة مطابقة لأمور مختلفة لأن الصورة الواحدة لو طابقت أموراً مختلفة لكانت مساوية في الماهية لتلك الأمور المختلفة فيكون لتلك الصورة حقائق مختلفة فلا تكون صورة واحدة (بل) يجب أن يكون
Then he takes to elaborating it, i.e., elaborating the answer, so he observes its detailing by observing its parts one after another. So in his mind at the moment he was asked there is a simple thing, which is the beginning of the details that occur in the second moment. And the distinction between that state, which occurs instantly after the question, and the state of ignorance, which was established before the question, and the observation of detailing that branches from elaboration is necessary and intuitive. For in the state of ignorance, called by the intellect potentiality, the perception of the answer is not actually present; rather, the soul in that state has the power to bring it to mind and detail it without exerting new acquisition. So there is pure potentiality. And in the state occurring after the question, there has actually occurred a feeling and some knowledge of the answer that was not present before it. And in the detailed state, the parts become intentionally observed, and that was not present in either of the two preceding states. And he likens that to someone who sees many livestock: sometimes all at once, for in this state he sees all its parts necessarily, and sometimes by fixing the gaze toward one by one, so he distinguishes them and separates its parts from each other. So the first vision is total vision, and the second is detailed vision, and the difference between them is known by intuition. So measure the state of the inner sight with respect to its objects to the state of the eye with respect to its objects in the existence of these two states in it as well. And Imam al-Razi said in denial of total knowledge: 'It is impossible for a single image to correspond to different things because if the single image corresponded to different things, it would be equal in quiddity to those different things, so that image would have different realities, so it would not be a single image. Rather, it must be...'
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and proof of the three states of knowledge regarding an answer: ignorance, the simple state (the beginning of details), and the detailed state. The author argues that these distinctions are intuitive. He provides an analogy to seeing many livestock: seeing them all at once is total vision, seeing them one by one is detailed vision. Then he cites Imam al-Razi's objection to the concept of total knowledge. Al-Razi argues that a single mental image cannot correspond to multiple different things because that would require the image to have multiple quiddities, which is impossible. The objection is incomplete as the text cuts off.

رؤية تفصيلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simple state is already detailed knowledge.
Correction: The simple state is only a beginning of details; it is potential, not fully actualized. The detailed state involves intentional observation of parts.

Checkpoint

A) Seeing each animal one by one.
B) Seeing the entire herd at once.
C) Not seeing at all.
D) Imagining the herd.

Key Takeaway
The three states of knowledge — ignorance, simple awareness, and detailed understanding — are intuitive and can be illustrated by the analogy of seeing livestock.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Regarding your knowledge of Allah's essence and attributes, are you content with a vague, total awareness (علم إجمالي), or do you actively strive for detailed understanding (علم تفصيلي)? What concrete step will you take this week to move from one state to the next?
?
Al-Razi doubted that a single mental image can represent multiple things. Do you ever settle for a superficial 'image' of a religious truth without examining its details? How might that be a form of intellectual laziness you need to confront?
?
The soul, in the state of ignorance, still has the power to bring knowledge to mind. What truth about your own character—perhaps a sin you ignore or a duty you neglect—do you have the power to bring to detailed awareness but have not yet done so? When will you stop avoiding it?

Refuting Summary Knowledge After a Question
Refutes summary knowledge claim with reality/concomitant distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a thing's reality and its concomitant, and why the claim of summary knowledge after a question is false.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge is actually detailed knowledge of a concomitant.

This passage addresses a common objection in Islamic logic and epistemology: that after hearing a question, one already has a vague, summary knowledge of the answer. The author refutes this by distinguishing two aspects of the answer: its true reality and its necessary concomitant. Understanding this distinction is key to clarifying what exactly we know when we formulate an answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
Pay attention to the key terms: حقيقة, ماهية, لازم.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The opponent claims that after a question, one has summary (ijmali) knowledge of the answer, not detailed (tafsili). Note the reason given: because the answer depends on mental preparation (taqrīr).
This is the position the author rejects.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between the answer's reality/essence (haqiqa/mahiyya) and its concomitant (lazim), which is the property of being suitable as an answer. The claim is that what is known after the question is the concomitant, not the reality.
The concomitant is known in detail, not summarily.
4
Examine the analogy with the soul
We know that the soul moves the body—this is detailed knowledge of a property. But the soul's true essence is unknown until discovered. Similarly, after a question, we know the answer's suitability in detail, not its full content.
The analogy reinforces the distinction and shows that the so-called 'summary' knowledge is actually detailed.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The refutation invalidates the opponent's claim. Additionally, it shows that a single act of knowledge cannot encompass multiple distinct known things; what was thought to be summary knowledge is actually a detailed knowledge of one aspect.
This has implications for epistemology and the nature of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما ما قالوه من أنه عقيب السؤال عالم بالجواب إجمالاً لا تفصيلاً لترتبه على التقرير فمردود بأن لذلك الجواب حقيقة وماهية وله لازم وهو أنه شيء يصلح جواباً لذلك السؤال والمعلوم عقيب السؤال هو ذلكم اللازم، وهو معلوم بالتفصيل، وأما الحقيقة فهي مجهولة في تلك الحالة، ونظير ذلك أنا إذا عرفنا النفس من حيث أنها شيء يحرك البدن فإن لازمها، أعني كونها محركة معلومة تفصيلا وحقيقتها مجهولة إلى أن تعرف بطريق آخر فبطل ما قالوه، وظهر أيضاً أنَّ العلم الواحد لا يكون علماً بمعلومات كثيرة
He said: As for what they said—that after the question one knows the answer summarily not in detail because it depends on the mental preparation (at-taqrīr)—it is rejected because that answer has a reality (ḥaqīqa) and essence (māhiyya), and it has a concomitant (lāzim), which is that it is something suitable as an answer to that question. What is known after the question is that concomitant, and it is known in detail. As for the reality, it is unknown in that state. An analogy for that is when we know the soul as something that moves the body; its concomitant, i.e., its being a mover, is known in detail, and its reality is unknown until it is known through another way. Thus what they said is invalidated, and it also becomes clear that a single knowledge cannot be knowledge of multiple known things.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection claims that after hearing a question, one has summary (not detailed) knowledge of the answer, because the answer depends on mental preparation (taqrīr). The refutation distinguishes between the answer's reality (its actual content) and a concomitant (lāzim) of it—the property of being suitable as an answer. What one actually knows after the question is that concomitant, and this knowledge is detailed, not summary. The reality of the answer remains unknown until discovered. An analogy with knowledge of the soul: we may know that the soul moves the body (a detailed concomitant), but its true essence is unknown until acquired through investigation. Therefore, the claim that summary knowledge of the answer exists is false. Furthermore, this shows that a single act of knowledge cannot encompass multiple distinct known things; the so-called 'summary knowledge' is actually a detailed knowledge of one aspect.

Key Terms
حقيقة
ماهية
لازم
علم إجمالي
علم تفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can have a vague, non-detailed knowledge of something right after a question.
Correction: What is known is actually a detailed knowledge of the answer's concomitant (suitability), not a summary knowledge of the answer's reality.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in the passage, what precisely is known after a question about the answer?
A) The answer's entire reality
B) A concomitant of the answer, such as its suitability as an answer
C) A vague summary
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
After a question, we know the answer's concomitant in detail, not its reality; thus, so-called summary knowledge is actually detailed knowledge of one aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The analogy of the soul shows you can know an effect (it moves the body) without knowing its essence. What aspects of your beliefs do you hold only by their outward signs, while the true reality remains unexplored? Are you content with that, or will you seek deeper understanding?
?
This passage exposes a common intellectual laziness: assuming we know something summarily when we actually only know one of its properties. Where in your own thinking have you been satisfied with 'summary' knowledge that masks a lack of real understanding? How will you change that starting today?

Knowing by an Aspect vs. Knowing the Aspect Itself
Distinguishes two types of knowledge and refutes composition of intellection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between knowing a thing by an aspect and knowing the aspect itself, and why conceptual knowledge of a relation can exist without assertive knowledge.

Core Idea
Conceptual knowledge of a relation can exist without assertive knowledge.

This passage clarifies a key epistemological distinction: knowing something by one of its aspects is different from knowing that aspect itself. This distinction underpins why acquisition in assents (taṣdīqāt) is possible. The commentator also addresses a misconception about intellection being composed of multiple forms, citing Avicenna's view that the intellect is simple.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic from 'وقوله : المعلوم عقيب السؤال' to 'ولا خفاء في أن كلامه يدل على أنَّ العلم البسيط كالخلاف للعلم'. Let the words settle before interpreting.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Look for the terms 'العلم بالشيء بالوجه' and 'العلم بالوجه'. Understand what each means.
3
Understand the reason for acquisition in assents
Find the sentence starting with 'ولذا لم ينكر الاكتساب في التصديقات' and note why it is not denied.
The relation (nisba) allows conceptual knowledge without assertive knowledge.
4
Examine the criticism of composed intellection
The passage says 'هذا مخالف لما في الشفاء'—identify what is being criticized and what Avicenna's view is.
Avicenna holds the intellect is simple with no multiplicity.
5
Reflect on simple knowledge and the active intellect
Read the quote from Al-Shifā about simple knowledge being one from which forms emanate. Consider the distinction between active knowledge and psychic knowledge.

وقوله : المعلوم عقيب السؤال بين العلم بالشيء بالوجه والعلم بالوجه وإن كان كون الشيء الواحد متصوراً بوجه دون وجه، ولذا لم ينكر الاكتساب في التصديقات لوجود النسبة التي يتعلق بها العلم التصوري والعلم التصديقي، فيجوز أن تكون معلومة تصوراً مجهولة تصديقاً. قوله : ( مركبة من صور (متعددة هذا مخالف لما في الشفاء من أنه عقل بسيط لا تكثر فيه أصلاً وأن تعقل الواجب، والمفارقات من هذا القبيل والفرق أنه عين الذات فيها وزائد على الذات فينا كما مر منقولاً عن المباحثات والظاهر أنه بسيط متعلق بالكل من حيث هو كل، ويجوز أن يكون العارض بسيطاً دون المعروض كالوحدة والتأليف والأطراف، نعم إن حصوله موقوف على حصول الأجزاء وصيرورتها مخزونة عند النفس كما يشهد به الوجدان . قوله : ( كان العلم بها على وجه أقوى الخ) في الشفاء الثاني : العلم البسيط الذي ليس من شأنه أن يكون له في نفسه صورة بعد صورة، لكن هو واحد يفيض منه الصور في قابل الصور فذلك علم فاعل للشيء الذي نسميه علماً فكرياً ومبدأ له، وذلك هو القوة العقلية من النفس المتشاكلة للعقول الفعالة وأما التفصيل فهو للنفس من حيث هو نفس فما لم يكن له ذلك لم يكن له علم نفساني، انتهى . ولا خفاء في أن كلامه يدل على أنَّ العلم البسيط كالخلاف للعلم. قوله : (فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم قيل للإمام أن يقول : فالتفاوت راجع إلى إخطار لا جزاء وعدمه وبهذا التقدر لا وجه لتقسيم العلم إلى الإجمالي والتفصيلي، كيف الإخطار وعدمه يجريان في البسائط أيضاً مع أن الظاهر أنهم لا يقولون بانقسام العلم بها إلى ذينك القسمين، وأما قوله : الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلاً في الذهن بحقيقته لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه ففيه أنه لا يصحح الدليل الذي تمسك به المستدل لأن العلم بالاضافة قد يحصل مع العلم بكلا طرفيها بوجه ولا يجوز أن يحمل كلامه على أن العلم بهذه الاضافة المخصوصة يتوقف على العلم بحقيقة الجواب، إذ لا يحصل العلم بالقدرة على ذلك بدون العلم بحقيقته ولو إجمالاً، لأن قوله : بكلا طرفيها يمنعه كما لا يخفى
And his statement: 'the known after the question' distinguishes between knowing a thing by an aspect (bi-l-wajh) and knowing the aspect itself (al-ʿilm bi-l-wajh), even though the one thing can be conceived in one aspect without another. For this reason, acquisition in assents (at-taṣdīqāt) was not denied, because of the existence of the relation (an-nisba) to which conceptual knowledge (al-ʿilm at-taṣawwurī) and assertive knowledge (al-ʿilm at-taṣdīqī) pertain. Thus it is permissible that it be known conceptually (taṣawwuran) but unknown assertively (taṣdīqan). His statement: 'composed of multiple forms'—this contradicts what is in Al-Shifā' that it is a simple intellectual (ʿaql basīṭ) without any multiplicity, and that the intellection of the Necessary and the separate intellects is of this kind, the difference being that it is identical to the essence in them and additional to the essence in us, as mentioned quoted from al-Mubāḥathāt. It appears that it is simple, relating to the whole as a whole. It is possible for the accident (al-ʿāriḍ) to be simple while the substrate is not, like unity, composition, and extremities. Yes, its occurrence depends on the occurrence of the parts and their becoming stored in the soul, as introspection (al-wijdān) attests. His statement: 'The knowledge of them was in a stronger way, etc.'—in the second part of Al-Shifā': The simple knowledge (al-ʿilm al-basīṭ) which is not such that it has in itself one form after another, but rather it is one from which forms emanate into the receiver of forms, that is an active knowledge of the thing which we call intellectual knowledge (ʿilman fikriyyan) and its principle. That is the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-ʿaqliyya) of the soul resembling the active intellects. As for detail, it belongs to the soul insofar as it is soul. If it did not have that, it would not have psychic knowledge (ʿilm nafsānī). End. It is not hidden that his speech indicates that simple knowledge is like the opposite of knowledge. His statement: 'Thus it became clear that the state of knowledge may vary'—it is said to the Imam that he may say: 'The variation goes back to the bringing to mind (al-ikhtār) and its absence. By this estimation, there is no basis for dividing knowledge into summary and detailed. How so? Bringing to mind and its absence also occur in simple things, while it is apparent that they do not say that knowledge of them is divided into those two divisions. As for his statement: 'The discussion is when the composite is present in the mind by its reality, not by virtue of any of its accidents,' it does not validate the proof that the reasoner clung to, because knowledge of a relation (al-ʿilm bi-l-iḍāfa) may be obtained alongside knowledge of both its ends (kullā ṭarafayhā) by some aspect, and it is not permissible to interpret his statement as that knowledge of this particular relation depends on knowledge of the reality of the answer, since knowledge of the ability to do so is not obtained without knowledge of its reality, even summarily, because his statement 'by both its ends' prevents it, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This section is a series of commentaries on previous phrases from the earlier discussion. First, it explains the distinction between knowing a thing by a certain aspect (bi-l-wajh) and knowing that aspect itself (al-ʿilm bi-l-wajh). This distinction is important for acquisition in assents (taṣdīqāt): conceptual knowledge of a relation can exist without assertive knowledge. Then it addresses the phrase 'composed of multiple forms' and contrasts it with Avicenna's view in Al-Shifā' that the intellect is simple without multiplicity, especially with regard to the Necessary Being and separate intellects. It points out that in us, the intellectual act is additional to the essence. It also mentions that the occurrence of such knowledge depends on the parts being stored in the soul. Next, it quotes Al-Shifā' on the nature of simple knowledge and active intellect. Finally, an objection is raised: if the variation in knowledge is only due to bringing to mind (ikhtār) or not, then even simple things could have summary and detailed knowledge, which is not accepted. The objection also argues that the proof used by the reasoner (mustadill) does not hold because knowledge of a relation can accompany knowledge of both its ends to some extent, and it is not necessary to know the reality of the answer to have knowledge of the ability. The commentator concludes that the objection prevents the inference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that intellection must be composed of multiple forms.
Correction: Avicenna argues that the intellect is simple and without multiplicity, especially for the Necessary Being and separate intellects.

Checkpoint
Why is acquisition in assents (at-taṣdīqāt) not denied according to the passage?
A) Because assents are always known
B) Because conceptual knowledge of a relation can exist without assertive knowledge
C) Because the intellect is simple
D) Because acquisition is impossible

Key Takeaway
Knowing a thing by an aspect does not entail knowing that aspect itself; this explains how conceptual knowledge can exist without assertive knowledge, and why intellection is simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your knowledge of Allah, is it merely conceptual—you know the words 'Allah is one'—or do you have an assertive certainty that transforms your life? What is one area where you need to move from knowing by an aspect to true certainty?
?
The passage describes simple knowledge as active, emanating forms. Are you passively receiving knowledge or actively living it? Today, what truth about Allah can you turn from a concept into a reality that shapes your actions?
?
If your intellection of Allah were truly simple and without multiplicity, how would that change the way you worship? Are you content with fragmented knowledge of His attributes, or do you seek a unified vision of His majesty? What step will you take tonight to gain that unity?

The Objection: Variable Knowledge and Summary vs. Detailed Division
Raises an objection about variable knowledge and critiques a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that variation in knowledge states is only about attention (ikhtār), and why this challenges the division of knowledge into summary and detailed.

Core Idea
If knowledge variation is only about attention, even simple things would be divisible into summary/detailed—which is not accepted.

The commentator presents a sharp objection to a previous claim: if the difference between states of knowledge is merely a matter of bringing to mind (ikhtār) or not, then the widely accepted division into summary and detailed knowledge would apply even to simple concepts, contradicting scholarly consensus. The objection also critiques the proof that knowledge of a relation depends on knowing its relata.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read from 'قوله : (فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم' to the end of the passage. Understand the flow of the objection.
2
Identify the core objection
The objection begins with a possible reply from the Imam: 'التفاوت راجع إلى إخطار لا جزاء وعدمه'. Understand what this means.
3
See the challenge to summary/detailed division
The objection argues that if variation is only about ikhtār, then even simple things (basā'iṭ) would have summary and detailed knowledge, which scholars reject.
4
Examine the critique of the proof about relations
The objection says the reasoner's proof is invalid because knowledge of a relation can accompany knowledge of both ends, and the phrase 'by both its ends' prevents the interpretation that the answer's reality must be known.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : المعلوم عقيب السؤال بين العلم بالشيء بالوجه والعلم بالوجه وإن كان كون الشيء الواحد متصوراً بوجه دون وجه، ولذا لم ينكر الاكتساب في التصديقات لوجود النسبة التي يتعلق بها العلم التصوري والعلم التصديقي، فيجوز أن تكون معلومة تصوراً مجهولة تصديقاً. قوله : ( مركبة من صور (متعددة هذا مخالف لما في الشفاء من أنه عقل بسيط لا تكثر فيه أصلاً وأن تعقل الواجب، والمفارقات من هذا القبيل والفرق أنه عين الذات فيها وزائد على الذات فينا كما مر منقولاً عن المباحثات والظاهر أنه بسيط متعلق بالكل من حيث هو كل، ويجوز أن يكون العارض بسيطاً دون المعروض كالوحدة والتأليف والأطراف، نعم إن حصوله موقوف على حصول الأجزاء وصيرورتها مخزونة عند النفس كما يشهد به الوجدان . قوله : ( كان العلم بها على وجه أقوى الخ) في الشفاء الثاني : العلم البسيط الذي ليس من شأنه أن يكون له في نفسه صورة بعد صورة، لكن هو واحد يفيض منه الصور في قابل الصور فذلك علم فاعل للشيء الذي نسميه علماً فكرياً ومبدأ له، وذلك هو القوة العقلية من النفس المتشاكلة للعقول الفعالة وأما التفصيل فهو للنفس من حيث هو نفس فما لم يكن له ذلك لم يكن له علم نفساني، انتهى . ولا خفاء في أن كلامه يدل على أنَّ العلم البسيط كالخلاف للعلم. قوله : (فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم قيل للإمام أن يقول : فالتفاوت راجع إلى إخطار لا جزاء وعدمه وبهذا التقدر لا وجه لتقسيم العلم إلى الإجمالي والتفصيلي، كيف الإخطار وعدمه يجريان في البسائط أيضاً مع أن الظاهر أنهم لا يقولون بانقسام العلم بها إلى ذينك القسمين، وأما قوله : الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلاً في الذهن بحقيقته لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه ففيه أنه لا يصحح الدليل الذي تمسك به المستدل لأن العلم بالاضافة قد يحصل مع العلم بكلا طرفيها بوجه ولا يجوز أن يحمل كلامه على أن العلم بهذه الاضافة المخصوصة يتوقف على العلم بحقيقة الجواب، إذ لا يحصل العلم بالقدرة على ذلك بدون العلم بحقيقته ولو إجمالاً، لأن قوله : بكلا طرفيها يمنعه كما لا يخفى

Commentary
This section is a series of commentaries on previous phrases from the earlier discussion. First, it explains the distinction between knowing a thing by a certain aspect (bi-l-wajh) and knowing that aspect itself (al-ʿilm bi-l-wajh). This distinction is important for acquisition in assents (taṣdīqāt): conceptual knowledge of a relation can exist without assertive knowledge. It points out that in us, the intellectual act is additional to the essence. It also mentions that the occurrence of such knowledge depends on the parts being stored in the soul. Next, it quotes Al-Shifā' on the nature of simple knowledge and active intellect. The objection also argues that the proof used by the reasoner (mustadill) does not hold because knowledge of a relation can accompany knowledge of both its ends to some extent, and it is not necessary to know the reality of the answer to have knowledge of the ability. The commentator concludes that the objection prevents the inference.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division of knowledge into summary and detailed applies to all types of knowledge, including simple concepts.
Correction: The objection shows that if variation were only about attention, even simple things would have such a division, but scholars do not accept that.

Checkpoint
What argument does the objection use to challenge the division of knowledge into summary and detailed?
A) The division is arbitrary
B) Bringing to mind and its absence also occur in simple things, yet scholars do not divide knowledge of them into those two divisions
C) Summary knowledge is impossible
D) The variation does not exist

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage warns against assuming a principle (attention-based division) without testing its boundaries. Where in your faith have you adopted a neat categorization that might not apply to the simplest truths about Allah? Will you examine your categories and ask Allah for a deeper understanding that respects His simplicity?
?

Is your 'knowledge of Allah' merely a mental concept (attention) while your heart remains disconnected? What one step can you take today to unite your knowledge with your action?

Objection: Division Applies to Simples
First objection: division based on bringing parts to mind applies to simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector claims that the division into total and detailed knowledge cannot be based on bringing parts to mind, because simples would also be included.

Core Idea
The division into total and detailed knowledge cannot rest solely on bringing parts to mind.

Sanusi presents an objection from the Imam: if the difference between total and detailed knowledge is merely whether parts are brought to mind, then this division would also apply to simple objects, which is not accepted. This forces a refinement of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how the objector frames the argument.
The objector assumes the definition of variation is bringing parts to mind.
2
Identify the flaw
The objector points out that simples (objects without parts) would also need to be divided, but scholars do not divide knowledge of them.
This shows the definition is too broad.
3
Consider the implication
Think about why this objection challenges the earlier division. It forces a more precise definition of total and detailed knowledge.
The response will come in later passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم قيل للإمام أن يقول : فالتفاوت راجع إلى إخطار أجزاء وعدمه وبهذا التقدير لا وجه لتقسيم العلم إلى الإجمالي والتفصيلي، كيف الإخطار وعدمه يجريان في البسائط أيضا مع أن الظاهر أنهم لا يقولون بانقسام العلم بها إلى ذينك القسمين، وأما قوله : الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه ففيه أنه لا يصحح الدليل الذي تمسك به المستدل لأن العلم بالإضافة قد يحصل مع العلم بكلا طرفيها بوجه ولا يجوز أن يحمل كلامه على أن العلم بهذه الإضافة المخصوصة يتوقف على العلم بحقيقة الجواب، إذ لا يحصل العلم بالقدرة على ذلك بدون العلم بحقيقته ولو إجمالا، لأن قوله : بكلا طرفيها يمنعه كما لا يخفى . قلنا الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته، لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه، فإن ذلك ليس علما بأجزائه لا تفصيلا ولا إجمالا، وأما قوله : العلم الواحد لا يكون علما بمعلومات كثيرة، فجوابه : أنا إذا قلنا : كل شيء فهو ممكن بالإمكان العام فلا شك أنا حكمنا على جميع أفراد الشيء، فلا بد أن تكون معلومة لنا، ولا علم بها في هذه الحالة إلا باعتبار مفهوم الشيء الشامل لها بأسرها، فإن العقل جعل هذا المفهوم آلة لملاحظة تلك الأفراد حتى أمكنه الحكم عليها، وتلخيصه : إن المفهوم الكلي قد يلاحظ في نفسه، وبهذه الملاحظة يمكن الحكم عليه لا على أفراده وقد يجعل آلة ومرآة لملاحظة أفراده، فيصح حينئذ أن يحكم على تلك الأفراد دونه ولتكن هذه المعاني التي قررناها مضبوطة عندك، فإنها تنفعك في مواضع عديدة
His statement: 'So it appears that the state of knowledge varies' – it was said to the Imam that he might say: The variation reduces to the bringing to mind of parts or its absence, and on this estimation there is no basis for dividing knowledge into total and detailed, since bringing to mind and its absence occur also in simple objects while apparently they do not say that knowledge of them divides into these two kinds. And as for his statement: 'The discussion is when the composite is present in the mind in its essence, not in virtue of an accident from its accidents' – it contains that it does not validate the proof that the arguer used, because knowledge of a relation may occur together with knowledge of both of its terms in some way, and it is not permissible to interpret his words as meaning that knowledge of this particular relation depends on knowledge of the reality of the answer, since knowledge of the power over that is not obtained without knowledge of its reality, even if total, because his statement 'both of its terms' prevents it, as is not hidden. We say: The discussion is when the composite is present in the mind in its essence, not in virtue of an accident from its accidents, for that is not knowledge of its parts, neither detailed nor total. And as for his statement: 'One knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things' – its answer is that if we say 'Everything is possible in the general sense', there is no doubt that we have judged upon all individuals of the thing, so they must be known to us, and there is no knowledge of them in this state except by virtue of the concept of the thing that comprises them all. For the intellect made this concept an instrument for observing those individuals until it became possible to judge upon them. Its summary: The universal concept may be observed in itself, and by this observation it is possible to judge upon it, not upon its individuals, and it may be made an instrument and mirror for observing its individuals, so then it becomes correct to judge upon those individuals without it. And let these meanings that we have established be fixed with you, for they benefit you in many places.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It first states an objection: if the difference between total and detailed knowledge is merely the bringing to mind (ikhtār) of parts or its absence, then this division would also apply to simple objects, which is not accepted. Additionally, the objection argues that the arguer's claim about the composite being known in its essence does not validate the proof, because knowledge of a relation can occur without knowledge of the reality of the answer. The response (from 'قلنا') clarifies that the discussion is specifically about the composite in its essence, not in terms of an accident, and that such accidental knowledge is not knowledge of parts at all. Then it addresses another objection: that a single knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things. The answer explains that when we judge 'everything is possible', we are using the universal concept 'thing' as an instrument (āla) and mirror (mirʼāt) to observe all individuals, so the one concept allows judgment on many individuals. The summary distinguishes two uses of the universal concept: observed in itself to judge upon itself alone, or used as a mirror to judge upon its individuals.

Key Terms
إخطار الأجزاء
البسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the objector argue that the division into total and detailed is unfounded?
A) Because it applies to simples as well
B) Because knowledge varies
C) Because the Imam said so
D) Because it is not based on parts

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into total and detailed cannot be adequately grounded solely on the criterion of bringing parts to mind, as simples would be included inappropriately.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah, do you ever unconsciously treat Him as a composite concept, mentally pulling apart His attributes? This lesson's objection shows that a method based on 'bringing parts to mind' fails for simples. How might you purify your knowledge of Allah from such mental divisions?
?
Have you ever used a framework to understand something, only to realize it doesn't apply to the simplest truths? In what area of your life might you be relying on an inadequate criterion, and what would it cost you to seek a deeper foundation?

Objection: Knowledge of Relation and Response
Second objection: knowledge of relation does not require detailed knowledge; response emphasizes essence vs. accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection concerning knowledge of a relation and the response that the composite must be known in its essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a relation does not require detailed knowledge of the terms; the composite must be known in essence.

A second objection challenges the proof by arguing that knowledge of a relation can occur without knowledge of the reality of the answer. The response distinguishes between knowledge of a composite in its essence versus in an accidental aspect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage that includes both the objection and the response starting with 'قلنا'. Follow the argument.
The objection quotes Sanusi's earlier condition.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says knowledge of a relation can happen with some knowledge of both terms, so the proof is not valid.
The key phrase is 'العلم بالإضافة قد يحصل مع العلم بكلا طرفيها'.
3
Grasp the response
The response ('قلنا') clarifies that the discussion is about the composite known in its essence, not in an accidental aspect. Accidental knowledge is not knowledge of its parts at all.
This sharpens the definition of 'knowledge of the composite'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم قيل للإمام أن يقول : فالتفاوت راجع إلى إخطار أجزاء وعدمه وبهذا التقدير لا وجه لتقسيم العلم إلى الإجمالي والتفصيلي، كيف الإخطار وعدمه يجريان في البسائط أيضا مع أن الظاهر أنهم لا يقولون بانقسام العلم بها إلى ذينك القسمين، وأما قوله : الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه ففيه أنه لا يصحح الدليل الذي تمسك به المستدل لأن العلم بالإضافة قد يحصل مع العلم بكلا طرفيها بوجه ولا يجوز أن يحمل كلامه على أن العلم بهذه الإضافة المخصوصة يتوقف على العلم بحقيقة الجواب، إذ لا يحصل العلم بالقدرة على ذلك بدون العلم بحقيقته ولو إجمالا، لأن قوله : بكلا طرفيها يمنعه كما لا يخفى . قلنا الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته، لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه، فإن ذلك ليس علما بأجزائه لا تفصيلا ولا إجمالا، وأما قوله : العلم الواحد لا يكون علما بمعلومات كثيرة، فجوابه : أنا إذا قلنا : كل شيء فهو ممكن بالإمكان العام فلا شك أنا حكمنا على جميع أفراد الشيء، فلا بد أن تكون معلومة لنا، ولا علم بها في هذه الحالة إلا باعتبار مفهوم الشيء الشامل لها بأسرها، فإن العقل جعل هذا المفهوم آلة لملاحظة تلك الأفراد حتى أمكنه الحكم عليها، وتلخيصه : إن المفهوم الكلي قد يلاحظ في نفسه، وبهذه الملاحظة يمكن الحكم عليه لا على أفراده وقد يجعل آلة ومرآة لملاحظة أفراده، فيصح حينئذ أن يحكم على تلك الأفراد دونه ولتكن هذه المعاني التي قررناها مضبوطة عندك، فإنها تنفعك في مواضع عديدة
His statement: 'So it appears that the state of knowledge varies' – it was said to the Imam that he might say: The variation reduces to the bringing to mind of parts or its absence, and on this estimation there is no basis for dividing knowledge into total and detailed, since bringing to mind and its absence occur also in simple objects while apparently they do not say that knowledge of them divides into these two kinds. And as for his statement: 'The discussion is when the composite is present in the mind in its essence, not in virtue of an accident from its accidents' – it contains that it does not validate the proof that the arguer used, because knowledge of a relation may occur together with knowledge of both of its terms in some way, and it is not permissible to interpret his words as meaning that knowledge of this particular relation depends on knowledge of the reality of the answer, since knowledge of the power over that is not obtained without knowledge of its reality, even if total, because his statement 'both of its terms' prevents it, as is not hidden. We say: The discussion is when the composite is present in the mind in its essence, not in virtue of an accident from its accidents, for that is not knowledge of its parts, neither detailed nor total. And as for his statement: 'One knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things' – its answer is that if we say 'Everything is possible in the general sense', there is no doubt that we have judged upon all individuals of the thing, so they must be known to us, and there is no knowledge of them in this state except by virtue of the concept of the thing that comprises them all. For the intellect made this concept an instrument for observing those individuals until it became possible to judge upon them. Its summary: The universal concept may be observed in itself, and by this observation it is possible to judge upon it, not upon its individuals, and it may be made an instrument and mirror for observing its individuals, so then it becomes correct to judge upon those individuals without it. And let these meanings that we have established be fixed with you, for they benefit you in many places.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It first states an objection: if the difference between total and detailed knowledge is merely the bringing to mind (ikhtār) of parts or its absence, then this division would also apply to simple objects, which is not accepted. Additionally, the objection argues that the arguer's claim about the composite being known in its essence does not validate the proof, because knowledge of a relation can occur without knowledge of the reality of the answer. The response (from 'قلنا') clarifies that the discussion is specifically about the composite in its essence, not in terms of an accident, and that such accidental knowledge is not knowledge of parts at all. Then it addresses another objection: that a single knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things. The answer explains that when we judge 'everything is possible', we are using the universal concept 'thing' as an instrument (āla) and mirror (mirʼāt) to observe all individuals, so the one concept allows judgment on many individuals. The summary distinguishes two uses of the universal concept: observed in itself to judge upon itself alone, or used as a mirror to judge upon its individuals.

Key Terms
العلم بالإضافة
المركب
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a relation always requires detailed knowledge of the terms involved.
Correction: Knowledge of a relation can occur with partial or general knowledge of the terms; the discussion is about knowledge of the composite in its essence, not accidental knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what kind of knowledge of the composite is the discussion about?
A) Accidental knowledge
B) Knowledge of its essence
C) Knowledge of its parts only
D) Knowledge of its accidents

Key Takeaway
The discussion of knowledge of a composite applies only when it is known in its essence, not in an accidental aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you know the core truths of your faith (like Tawhid) in their essence, or only through accidental attributes like rituals and customs? How would you test your knowledge today?
?
When you seek knowledge of Allah, do you settle for knowing about Him through relations (e.g., 'He is Merciful') without delving into the essential reality of His attributes? What step will you take this week to move from accidental to essential knowledge?
?
Reflect on a time you were content with surface-level knowledge of a religious matter. How did that affect your certainty? Can you identify one area where you need to grasp the essence, not just the accident, and commit to studying it deeply?

One Knowledge Knowing Many Things
Third objection: one knowledge cannot know many; response via universal concept as instrument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single universal concept can provide knowledge of many things by serving as an instrument.

Core Idea
A universal concept can be a mirror to observe many individuals, allowing one knowledge to cover many.

The final objection claims that one knowledge cannot know many things. The response shows that when we make a universal judgment, the concept acts as an instrument to reach all individuals. This is a crucial principle for kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage that includes the objection and the full answer. Note the example of 'everything is possible'.
The answer contains a summary that distinguishes two uses of the universal concept.
2
Understand the two uses of the universal concept
The universal concept can be observed in itself (to judge upon it) or used as an instrument/mirror to observe its individuals (to judge upon them).
This is the key insight.
3
Apply the principle
Think of other examples where a single universal concept allows judgment on many particulars, such as 'all humans are mortal'.
This principle is useful in many theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم قيل للإمام أن يقول : فالتفاوت راجع إلى إخطار أجزاء وعدمه وبهذا التقدير لا وجه لتقسيم العلم إلى الإجمالي والتفصيلي، كيف الإخطار وعدمه يجريان في البسائط أيضا مع أن الظاهر أنهم لا يقولون بانقسام العلم بها إلى ذينك القسمين، وأما قوله : الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه ففيه أنه لا يصحح الدليل الذي تمسك به المستدل لأن العلم بالإضافة قد يحصل مع العلم بكلا طرفيها بوجه ولا يجوز أن يحمل كلامه على أن العلم بهذه الإضافة المخصوصة يتوقف على العلم بحقيقة الجواب، إذ لا يحصل العلم بالقدرة على ذلك بدون العلم بحقيقته ولو إجمالا، لأن قوله : بكلا طرفيها يمنعه كما لا يخفى . قلنا الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته، لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه، فإن ذلك ليس علما بأجزائه لا تفصيلا ولا إجمالا، وأما قوله : العلم الواحد لا يكون علما بمعلومات كثيرة، فجوابه : أنا إذا قلنا : كل شيء فهو ممكن بالإمكان العام فلا شك أنا حكمنا على جميع أفراد الشيء، فلا بد أن تكون معلومة لنا، ولا علم بها في هذه الحالة إلا باعتبار مفهوم الشيء الشامل لها بأسرها، فإن العقل جعل هذا المفهوم آلة لملاحظة تلك الأفراد حتى أمكنه الحكم عليها، وتلخيصه : إن المفهوم الكلي قد يلاحظ في نفسه، وبهذه الملاحظة يمكن الحكم عليه لا على أفراده وقد يجعل آلة ومرآة لملاحظة أفراده، فيصح حينئذ أن يحكم على تلك الأفراد دونه ولتكن هذه المعاني التي قررناها مضبوطة عندك، فإنها تنفعك في مواضع عديدة

Rational Judgments
intermediate

Objection and answer on collective vs. detailed knowledge
Objection-and-answer refuting the distinctness of collective and detailed knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that collective knowledge and detailed knowledge are not two distinct types but two modes of the same knowledge referring to the same object.

Core Idea
Collective and detailed knowledge differ in mode, not in object.

In this passage, the author addresses a common misconception that collective knowledge and detailed knowledge are fundamentally different types. He argues that the object known is identical, and the difference lies in the knowledge itself being either total (incomplete) or detailed (complete). This distinction is crucial for understanding further discussions in epistemology.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Read the objection that someone thought collective and detailed knowledge are distinct types.
2
Identify the answer
Find the author's immediate response: the thing known is one, what differs is the knowledge.
3
Understand the clarification
Notice the author's explanation that totality and detail refer to the knowledge, not the object.
This refutes the misconception.
4
Connect to the second branch
See how the author shows that even the second branch depends on affirming collective knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد ظن أن العلم الجملي نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي والجواب : أنَّ الإجمال والتفصيل، ليس حالهما على ما توهمه بل المعلوم فيهما واحد والمختلف هو العلم المتعلق بذلك المعلوم، فتارة يكون ذلك العلم في نفسه على وجه، وأخرى على وجه آخر كما تحققته، فليس الإجمال بأن يكون الشيء معلوماً من وجه ومجهولاً من آخر، وإذا قلنا : هذا الشيء معلوم من حيث الإجمال دون التفصيل، كانت الحيثيتان راجعتين إلى العلم دون المعلوم، وبما قررناه يتضح أنَّ الفرع الثاني أيضاً فرع على ثبوت العلم الإجمالي كأنه قيل: هل هو من قبيل العلم بالشيء من وجه دون وجه أو 
قوله : (بل المعلوم فيهما واحد) وهو الأجزاء فإنها معلومة في الإجمالي علماً ناقصاً وفي التفصيلي علماً تاماً.
Someone thought that collective knowledge (العلم الجملي) is a type distinct from detailed knowledge (العلم التفصيلي). The answer: that totality and detail are not as he imagined; rather, the thing known in both is one, and what differs is the knowledge relating to that thing known. Sometimes that knowledge is in itself in one manner, and at other times in another manner, as you have verified. So totality is not that a thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another. And when we say: 'This thing is known in terms of totality without detail,' the two aspects refer back to the knowledge, not to the thing known. And by what we have established, it becomes clear that the second branch also branches from the affirmation of collective knowledge, as if it were said: Is it of the type of knowledge of a thing from one aspect and not from another, or? His saying: 'Rather, the thing known in both is one' — meaning the parts; they are known in the collective with incomplete knowledge, and in the detailed with complete knowledge.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. It begins by stating someone's mistaken belief that collective knowledge (العلم الجملي) and detailed knowledge (العلم التفصيلي) are two different types of knowledge. The author refutes this by clarifying that the thing known (المعلوم) is the same in both cases; what differs is the knowledge itself in its mode. Totality and detail are not properties of the object but of the knowledge. The author explains that when we say something is known in totality but not in detail, the 'totality' and 'detail' refer to the knower's cognition, not the object. The passage concludes that the second branch (a question about knowledge from one aspect) also depends on affirming collective knowledge. The marginal note glosses the phrase 'the thing known in both is one' by specifying that it refers to the parts, which are known incompletely in collective knowledge and completely in detailed knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم الجملي
العلم التفصيلي
الإجمال
التفصيل
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Collective knowledge and detailed knowledge are two distinct types of knowledge.
Correction: They are two modes of the same knowledge of a single object.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that collective knowledge and detailed knowledge are two distinct types?
A) They are distinct types.
B) The thing known is the same but the knowledge differs in mode.
C) They are identical in every respect.
D) The thing known is different in each case.

Key Takeaway
Collective and detailed knowledge are modes of the same knowledge of a single object, not separate types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your knowledge of fundamental Islamic beliefs, such as the oneness of Allah, do you possess it as collective knowledge (a general acceptance) or as detailed knowledge (deeply pondered and articulated)? Which mode do you genuinely rely upon when facing doubts? What would it take for you to transform your collective knowledge into detailed knowledge?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because you only knew it collectively and failed to appreciate its details? For example, the verse 'Indeed, with hardship comes ease' – have you truly detailed its meaning in your own life? How does this lesson urge you to move from vague acceptance to precise understanding?
?
The passage shows that the object of knowledge remains unchanged; only our mode of knowing varies. In your relationship with the Qur'an, are you satisfied with collective knowledge of its meanings, or do you strive for detailed knowledge? What concrete step can you take today to deepen your mode of knowing without altering the object?
